### Post by Dragonbite on 2009-02-12
Having an opinion for something, like Linux, is not difficult and it is easy to then demonize and put down the "competition", like Microsoft, at every turn.

Unfortunately this is also part of the criteria of a fanboy, or fanatic.

The question is, can you say something positive aboue Microsoft? Can you actually "see" from the other person's point of view?

Since there are so many arguments both for- and against-Microsoft there has to be some merit to both sides of the coin, but can you find it?

I've noticed a growing anti-Microsoft feeling but I wonder if it is blind following the herd like sheep or are you actually making a choice?

---

### Post by Dixon Bainbridge on 2009-02-12
Ive switched back to XP from ubuntu on my home machine as linux doesnt cut it for the apps I need.

XP is a decent, stable, quick OS when you strip it all down. I uninstall every MS app and component I can and replace them with 3rd party alternatives. XP runs great then.

XP is faster than OSX.

I like the fact that every app I need has an XP client which works great.

I have no problems with MS behaving like a business. Businesses look to screw everyone, thats how they make money. If it wasnt MS at no1 then it'd be someone else.

I think Steve Ballmer is funny.

I think Bill Gates is a top man for his foundation and charity work.

The more I use linux on the desktop, the more I see its limitations. It desperately needs 3rd party commercial apps.

### Post by RiceMonster on 2009-02-12
> **Regnulify said:**

while I prefer to use an editor like vim, I'll admit Visual Studio is a very good IDE. It's got really good debugging tools. I think NetBeans is a pretty close competitor to it, even though it's slow as hell.

### Post by Dragonbite on 2009-02-12
> **RiceMonster said:**
> You can write regular native C/C++ in Visual Studio. You can also write assembly in it. I've used it for both of those very recently. It doesn't have to be .NET

while I prefer to use an editor like vim, I'll admit Visual Studio is a very good IDE. It's got really good debugging tools. I think NetBeans is a pretty close competitor to it, even though it's slow as hell.

I agree with Visual Studio being very good and NetBeans being the closer equivalent. Some of that is the ease of building the GUI and then associating what runs during an Event.

I've tried Monodevelop and it just doesn't cut it (for me). If Monodevelop or and Eclipse plug-in could reach the ease of VS I could see Mono applications and adoption increase significantly. I suspect that there would be a number of Windows developers willing to fool around in Mono if the tools were similar.

Office is definitely one of their strongest products and Office 2007, once you get past the learning curve, is pretty good.

I haven't fooled around with it too much lately but their Live applications (chat, (blog)Writer, Gallery, etc.) are surprisingly pretty good.  I would love to find a Linux app with the formatting, ease and multiple-blogging upload capabilities.

And last, for me, is that things generally "just work"; printer, scanner, webcam (assumption), tablet, etc.

I just prefer knowing more of what is going on my system, though, which leads me to Linux.

### Post by DarkOx on 2009-02-12
Upgrading applications is far easier on Windows.

For example, to upgrade to the latest version of Open Office on Windows, I go download the .exe and double-click it. To do the same on Ubuntu, I have to either:
a) Hope that it's backported
b) Hope that there's a .deb that works with hardy or
c) Upgrade my entire OS to the latest version

In the case of Open Office, I ended up downloading and installing about a billion separate .debs. Each individual .deb was as easy to install as the .exe on Windows, but overall installing was a pain. (Yes, I know you can do it quicker through the command-line, but if you don't know about dpkg, how are you supposed to discover that?)

Another example is Gnome-Do, where to install version 0.8, you need to be running Intrepid. On Windows, I don't have to upgrade my OS to run the latest applications (baring anything truly archaic, like Windows 98 ). Pretty much everything is still compatible with XP. Whereas on Ubuntu, Hardy is only a year out and already there's software I can't run on it.

I realize that most of this problem is outside of the Ubuntu project's control (it's up to the Open Office guys to package things, etc.), but that's not the point: bottom line is that it's easier to upgrade your applications on Windows.

### Post by mohitchawla on 2009-02-12
Like other things in life, you do something or use something (in our case, software) based on its philosophical/moral foundation apart from its practical implications and actual nature...so, knowing what ideals Microsoft is based on, I do not think it is necessary to even _think_ about its positives or make such threads. Now, counter-arguing what has been listed so far as positive things about Microsoft's products...well, necessity is the mother of invention, so I guess people from the free/open source community could have done all that (rather, would have done that), its just that the motivations of the two opposing communities were not the same and one came out with applications and software before the other and started selling it. And then the whole  "they have this, while you don't have this" business started and basically it all came down to being Windows-like or Microsoft-like which pisses off a lot of people. So if you are saying they brought the good from us, well I think the good was always there, they just made it come out faster and dirtier.

### Post by yse on 2009-02-12
I don't hate MS, here are my preferences:


**1. Vista/W7 overall ergonomy, runing on good PC**, nothing compare with that. Aero/font rendering/general UI features are way to nice. Rock solid. Yep, if you have a crap computer, you can skip that part. I prefer to have a top PC and enjoy all the new technologies features, instead trying to tweak an OS to run on a old hardware, what is just a waist of time.

**2. Visual Studio.** Yep, there is nothing to compare with that one. I use Eclipse/Kdevelop/Monodevelop on linux, they don't compare with VS. Even VS Express (free version) is better that Eclipse/Kdevelop.

**3. DirectX**. What else can i say.. Best games runs on it for a reason.

**4. Office**. No more comments. Who hate ribbon, i am sorry for you guys, you just hate MS and don't love new stuff.


And some words.. I've seen some peoples arguing about MS asking money for OS. Well, **give me a Linux distro who bring those new technologies on table AND I PAY FOR IT**. Seriously. Give something comparable for real and I PAY. And i am not the only one who will maybe. Until then, "fanbois" stop for a minute.

And don't forget, MS is pushing forward almost all hardware vendors with their technologies, not linux.

And yea, for all linux "fanbois" who will reply: Keeping the bad parts about linux hidden dont make next distro better. Better we show what we like, maybe next distro will be better.

### Post by jespdj on 2009-02-12
I am not a Microsoft hater. Besides Ubuntu I use Windows Vista (at home) and Windows XP (at work).

Microsoft is very good at marketing; Bill Gates is a very good business man. Windows is not the de-facto OS on almost all computers because it's technically superior, but because Bill Gates sold it to the whole world.

Microsoft is not so good at innovation. What they are good at is taking technology invented by others, building their own version and then adding proprietary stuff to it, and making everybody use their proprietary version. That's how Microsoft corrupts open standards. An example where they tried this and failed is with Sun's Java. Sun sued Microsoft and Microsoft lost. After that they made the .NET framework which is built on exactly the same concepts as Java EE. The .NET framework is not bad, but Microsoft didn't invent the concepts on which it is built.

Another thing that I don't like on Windows is that people ask money for even the silliest little programs. I was looking for a mass renaming tool once, and found a utility that cost $10. Likewise there is a Compiz-like desktop cube program for Windows that costs $25.

I love the free and open source philosophy of Linux. There is also open source software for Windows, but far less than there is for Linux.

The fact that Adobe programs run on Windows is ofcourse not Microsoft's merit. If Adobe would make Photoshop for Linux, I would buy it.

I can't think of anything in Windows that's really better than in Linux. Technically, Windows is not a very exciting OS.

Someone mentioned Visio - note that Visio wasn't originally developed by Microsoft. They bought the company that made Visio a few years ago.

Anyway, the origination of DOS is irrelevant to the fact that MS-DOS-based computers introduced the vast majority of the world's users to personal computing.

2:

Er, how about the prevalence of MS-DOS based business applications, games including the original Windows... How can MS being the operating system of choice on over 90% of all the world's computers not indicate to you that MS brought computing to the masses?

1:
It was indeed a good business strategy, making Microsoft loads of money.
But it was only possible for Bill Gates because IBM was under investigation. MS-DOS was not what introduced the world to computers, it was IBM and other hardware vendors.
MS-DOS contained absolutely nothing new or particular attractive, What was new was the IBM PC (16 bit and all that jazz).
I guess this was the lesson that taught Bill Gates that a good strategy is more important than a good product.

2:
because of before mentioned investigation IBM left the PC architecture open. This allowed the clones to transform the success of the PC into the defacto business standard, and even enter home computing.
Other popular systems before the PC could not be legally cloned.
The PC offered an unparalleled combination of Vendor backing and vendor independence. 

3:
Games came to the PC because it was huge, DOS sucked at games, but games just used DOS to load, and then took over the system.
Microsoft did very poorly at any applications for DOS.
Windows 1 and 286 and 386 sucked so badly nobody wanted it.
For some weird reason IBM thought it was a good idea to let Microsoft help develop OS/2, and with the knowledge and money gained from this, Microsoft finally had success with Windows 3.0. Microsoft back stabbed IBM with its own money and technology, and succeeded in winning the OS war.
Windows 3.0 was no wonder even at the time, you just had to look to either Apple or Commodore to find something better, except they were not PCs.

### Post by abyssius on 2009-02-12
Re: point 1: In business, a good marketing strategy is always more important than a good product. Ask Apple. If they rested entirely on their "good product" laurels, they wouldn't even have advanced to where they are today. No matter how you look at it, the IBM PC would be a chunk of metal and plastic without MS-DOS. IBM targeted businesses with DOS-based applications that solved problems. Commodore focused on being a toy.

Re: point 2:
History has proved that an open architecture stance as opposed to Apple's closed proprietary hardware/software position was the correct market move for the PC industry. MS also deserves credit for the "open architecture" policy you describe. Because, since MS still owned the OS, if they decided to keep it proprietary, IBM wouldn't have been able to implement this strategy.

Re: point 3: Blanket condemnations like "DOS sucked" really don't add anything intellectual to the debate. Also, if it were true that "nobody wanted" DOS/Windows then both MS and IBM would have went out of business. Nobody wanted Commodore that's why they finally failed. How can you make a statement like Apple and Commodore were not PC's??? How do you define a "PC"?

> Re: point 1: No matter how you look at it, the IBM PC would be a chunk of metal and plastic without MS-DOS. IBM targeted businesses with DOS-based applications that solved problems. Commodore focused on being a toy.
You are right, the IBM-PC needed an operating system. However, that doesn't translate to it being a chunk of metal and plastic without MS-DOS. This would only be true if MS-DOS had been the only game in town IBM could have chosen, which it wasn't or if MS-DOS had brought something unique to the IBM-PC, which it didn't. Case in point: Originally IBM thought about licensing C/PM, the very operating system that QDOS, the OS Microsoft bought, was a copy off.

Now, nobody says that MS didn't make a lot of brilliant business decisions, but I still fail to see and so far you have not been able to show how buying an operating system that was based on an other operating system and then licensing it for the platform that by no doing of MS became the defacto standard translates into bringing computing to the masses.

> **abyssius said:**
> 
Re: point 2:
History has proved that an open architecture stance as opposed to Apple's closed proprietary hardware/software position was the correct market move for the PC industry. MS also deserves credit for the "open architecture" policy you describe. Because, since MS still owned the OS, if they decided to keep it proprietary, IBM wouldn't have been able to implement this strategy.

Now you really get your history tangled up.
1. It was not IBM's strategy to have an open architecture. Compaq reverse-engineered the BIOS. That's what started the clones and thus the PC business.

2. What are you talking about when you say that MS had an "open architecture" and did not keep their OS proprietary? I honestly don't understand what you are trying to say.

> **abyssius said:**
> 
Re: point 3: Blanket condemnations like "DOS sucked" really don't add anything intellectual to the debate. Also, if it were true that "nobody wanted" DOS/Windows then both MS and IBM would have went out of business. Nobody wanted Commodore that's why they finally failed. How can you make a statement like Apple and Commodore were not PC's??? How do you define a "PC"?
First off, the poster you answering said that nobody wanted the first versions of Windows because they weren't very good. I think if you look at their market success or lack thereof he simply states a fact.

As to DOS: It simply wasn't a very advanced operating system even for its time. I don't think pointing this out does translate to simply stating "DOS sucked" and I think it's intellectually dishonest to simply state that MS and DOS brought computing to the masses without even trying to point out what it was that made DOS so unique and good that it became the standard OS not because of what others have mentioned before but because of its merits. So, please point out the technical merits of MS-DOS that made it unique and brought computing to the masses. And please explain how the competing DOSes fit into this.

Finally, if you want to know why people are saying that DOS wasn't very good, simply look up why companies like Compaq felt they had to build something that was hardware compatible with the IBM-PC though DOS was also available for other non-compatible platforms. One of the reasons for this, the main reason, was that DOS was so inadequate that people had to program directly to the hardware as the DOS-API simply didn't provide sufficiently advanced options.

### Post by Kazade on 2009-02-13
OK, I can't resist a challenge..

**Good Things about Microsoft**

They make good keyboards, mice and joysticks
Microsoft Word 2003 was awesome.. 2007 would be better if it weren't for the ribbon. It'll be even better with ODF support coming
Excel is good.
Samba seems to be quite a good thing.

**Bad Things about Microsoft**

Continuous vendor lock-in ( [http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vendor_lock-in#Microsoft](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vendor_lock-in#Microsoft) )

Illegal abuse of their monopoly 
( see the Opera lawsuit - before anyone mentions "how come they get into trouble when everyone else bundles a browser?" - 
a. They are a monopoly, rules are different. 
b. Try removing Internet Explorer from Windows. )

The whole OOXML debate. The flawed security model of Windows. The implementation and support of Digital Rights Management. The inability to follow any kind of standard properly. The MS Java VM embrace, extend, extinguish attempt (which eventually led to C#). The appalling installation process for Windows (e.g. messing up the MBR making other OSes unaccessible). The anti-Linux FUD they continue to spread ([http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microsoft#Misrepresentation](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microsoft#Misrepresentation)). The continuing bloating of Windows with each version. The list goes on and on.

Ultimately, most of the bad rep comes from their business practices, rather than their software. 

The way I see it, if the market was fair, if every PC sold had the option of any operating system without Microsoft's marketing machine influencing the whole thing, Windows would not stand a chance (in its current state) against the main Linux distros. It lags on features (apt, compiz, multiple desktops etc.), security, standards compatibility and (IMO)usability. Microsoft would then be forced to innovate. Unfortunately, that's not the case. I can continue to dream :)

### Post by BuffaloX on 2009-02-13
> **glotz said:**
> You guys *are* aware of the Micro$oft patent mine field surrounding the issue and that being the reason it hasn't yet been duplicated and surpassed on Free Software? #-o

Too bad they were 20 years late with their patent. :^o

[http://www.grc.com/ctwho.htm]("http://www.grc.com/ctwho.htm")

Unfortunately this patent has scared many open source developers from implementing sub pixel font rendering.
But honestly anyone working with graphics on computers would soon discover sub pixels, provided they had color monitors. Personally I discovered this in the mid 80's, which doesn't make me particular smart, it's just that at the time I fiddled quite a bit programming graphics on my Amiga, and investigating how pixels on my monitor interacted, using a magnifying glass, and comparing it to the RGB values in my code. As a programmer of color graphics, you are literally forced to work with sub-pixels, since everything is broken down to RGB values.
I didn't notice this on my C64, probably because it only had 16 colors, and RGB values wasn't available.
Seems Wozniac was there 10 years before me.
I never really used sub pixeling, because you need a different algorithm for all monitor pixel layouts in existence. Plus on a CRT monitor the resolution rarely matched the mask.
Today limiting sub pixeling to work on LCD monitors greatly simplifies the process, because sub pixels are always organized horizontally or if the monitor is rotated vertically, and there is only two types of order for the subpixels, limiting the needed algorithms for perfect rendering in each case to four, even then many rendering systems only works with horizontal RGB since this covers the vast majority.

Sorry simply had to get this off my chest. :P

I didn't notice this on my C64, probably because it only had 16 colors, and RGB values wasn't available.

On the C64, we used dithering techniques to make it appear to have more than 16 colors. So, I guess dithering was similar to the sub-pixel techniques only we were limited by a very low resolution.

Xbox uses ATI for graphics and Toshiba for the drives. The Zune uses Toshiba HDD's as well. What's your point?

All hardware manufacturers of every sort (not just computer related) outsource most of their components. Very few TV makers make their own screens, and even those ones have processors, memory and the like made by other companies. That's the nature of building hardware. That doesn't make it any less their product.

How many parts in an HP computer do you think are built in a HP manufacturing plant?

The Xbox line is fantastic, and it was brought to us by Microsoft. That's just the bottom line. If you want to split it up into individual components and criticize them for not building it from scratch then you have no business buying anything hardware related.

I'm willing to bet the RAM in your PC is labeled something like Kingston, OCZ, Corsair, Crucial, Mushkin, etc, but crack open any one of those and I'll bet the chips are labeled Samsung or Micron...

I'ts hypocritical to criticize this practice if you buy absolutely anything with electronics in it.

### Post by BuffaloX on 2009-02-13
> **cardinals_fan said:**
> By default.  The only way in which my customized XP install is insecure is that it depends solely on Microsoft for security updates.

You can make XP very secure as a personal desktop.
Problem is not all companies can make their system as secure, because they rely on some of the services that has vulnerabilities.

On a fresh install I used to disable more than 10 services, never used IE except for downloading Firefox, never used outlook or outlook express.
Never used MS Office, used pop-tray to check mail on the server so suspicious or irrelevant mails could be deleted directly on the server.
Installed Suns Java instead of MS Java. 
the firewall was set up in my router, because I don't trust that something couldn't override a firewall installed in Windows.

These steps cannot be done by an average user, an average user following generally sound procedures is still at risk of getting infected. I know because I've seen it happen soo many times.

### Post by BuffaloX on 2009-02-15
> **djsroknrol said:**
> Well, if the roles were reversed...would you buy Linux? 

Billy Boy was at the right place, at the right time and came to market with a product that the people needed....so it boils down to capitalism vs. FOSS.

Just my .02

Like Linux is a virtual monopoly that come pre-installed, Windows a small proprietary OS?

Who would make programs for Windows if it only had about 2 percent of the market?
Microsoft wouldn't have enough resources to make all the stuff they do today. There would be nothing attractive about Windows, and it would quickly die. Reversing the roles would mean the death of Microsoft.
Maybe Microsoft only use 1/10 th of their earnings on development, but if they only earn 1/50 th, they would need to use 5 times their earnings on development.
They wouldn't have nearly the level of hardware support they enjoy today. Everything would work better with Linux than Windows, because all vendors would contribute drivers. Apps would be developed for Linux.
Linux scales both up and down, everything from mobile phones to supercomputers, which segment would be open for windows to compete in?
Windows has no technological merit that would justify its existence.

Sorry I don't know how to reverse the roles without it all becoming paradoxical and impossible.

So I guess if the roles were reversed, I would use some vendor backed distro or Debian, since I suppose that in that reality Ubuntu would never have been created.

### Post by MikeTheC on 2009-02-15
> **BuffaloX said:**
> Sorry I don't know how to reverse the roles without it all becoming paradoxical and impossible.
In my Intro Algebra college class, we would call that a Contradiction or, possibly, an "Empty Set".

Microsoft was not started for the same reason as the Free Software Foundation. They exist on a continuum with some considerable philosophical distance between them.

Also, this whole notion of "Capitalism vs. F/OSS" is a straw man argument at best. F/OSS is a conduit, not a condition. Generally, the idea is to use F/OSS as a means of introducing a concept and having it improved by the community to an extent far greater than the originator could by him/herself alone. The general theory is that you make money from something else, like support or associated hardware, etc. Neither RMS nor Linus Torvalds nor Eric S. Raymond nor anyone else in the F/OSS movement is against people making money through their own work.

---

### Post by Perkins on 2009-02-16
Much as I disagree with some of their business practices (Like the occasional "accident" that kind of points to a total lack of ethics) Microsoft does do a few things right.

Mostly these few things consist of having hardware support for virtually anything PC related the day it comes out, if not before.  The tiny bits of things left over have to do with hiding the machinery behind the GUI in such a way that it doesn't intrude upon the peace and tranquility of the average, technopeasant user, without making it so inaccessable as to be completely unrepairable if something breaks.


Put it this way.  Bill Gates is quite good at figuring out what the average joe needs his OS to be able to do, and designing a system that will do everything for everyone out of the box.  But it quite often becomes a classic case of "jack of all trades, master of none."


Most of the problems they have, in my opinion, stem from the way they run their programming shops.  They split their people into teams, and whichever one delivers a working product first gets to keep their jobs.  Not the one who produces the best version, or the smoothest running version.  The *first* one.  As a consequence the code they write tends to sacrifice run-time efficiency and space efficiency for writing-time efficiency, and be full of unforseen flaws which must be corrected later (generally not until after the flaws have generated enough complaints that they're worried about being sued.)

Personally I like this guy's take on it all.  (computers and operating systems in general)  Good article, worth the time to read.

[http://www.cryptonomicon.com/beginning.html](http://www.cryptonomicon.com/beginning.html)

---

### Post by eneville on 2009-02-16
> **forrestcupp said:**
> Ah, the never ending argument.

I'd rather spend my time learning to write better code than waste it trying to learn a complex editor.

You'd write cleaner and better formatted code with a better editor that works for you rather than working for itself.

Vim gives us the power to create without needing to power the mouse. Think how much more efficient you'd be if you never had to read for the mouse to move around your code.

I found my productivity shot through the roof once I didn't have to move my hands from the keyboard.

One of the key design principles behind vim was to make it efficient. Being able to wrap commands within maps (aliases) allows you to whack out macros from single key strokes. This can apply to advanced features such as checking in, starting the compile sequence, debugging, grepping, or whatever else you want to pull in. You have the whole suite of unix tools attached to your key strokes. I think that's just way too good an offer to pass up.

One thing is better in Visual Studio than vim, and that's probably the dialog editor. But that's as far as I'd go with it. Everything else is sub optimal.

### Post by Kazade on 2009-02-17
> **eneville said:**
> You'd write cleaner and better formatted code with a better editor that works for you rather than working for itself.

Vim gives us the power to create without needing to power the mouse. Think how much more efficient you'd be if you never had to read for the mouse to move around your code.

I found my productivity shot through the roof once I didn't have to move my hands from the keyboard.

One of the key design principles behind vim was to make it efficient. Being able to wrap commands within maps (aliases) allows you to whack out macros from single key strokes. This can apply to advanced features such as checking in, starting the compile sequence, debugging, grepping, or whatever else you want to pull in. You have the whole suite of unix tools attached to your key strokes. I think that's just way too good an offer to pass up.

One thing is better in Visual Studio than vim, and that's probably the dialog editor. But that's as far as I'd go with it. Everything else is sub optimal.

Hmm.. OK, I don't want to start a flame war or anything but I can't resist replying :)

I use VIM regularly and have done for a few years, although I'm no expert. I've worked with people that are, and ultimately I don't think a user who is proficient in VIM (or Emacs) is much more efficient than someone who is proficient in some GUI IDE with their own keyboard shortcuts with a terminal alongside. 

It's all a matter of personal taste. I personally am really proficient with the normal keyboard shortcuts (combinations of CTRL, SHIFT, arrow keys, Home, End, PgUp, PgDown, function keys etc.) and I can navigate a document pretty quickly without the mouse. If I'm programming, my IDE provides keys for starting the debugger, setting breakpoints, compiling, setting watches, opening files etc. pretty much anything I need.

Granted I do miss the ability to do stuff like deleting up to a certain character. But the minor amount of efficiency I lose there is definitely made up in other ways (class wizards, symbols browsers, project managers, build settings dialogs etc.)

Me and my friend have this debate over and over, it really gets nowhere. It comes down to personal preference.

That said, MSVC is not the best IDE in the world BY FAR. The code completion sucks (and generates huge files alongside the project), I hate the way the settings are organized, the associated compiler doesn't even support C99. Code::Blocks is far nicer and I'm just discovering the amazingly simple and flexible plugin system which I'm using to fill any gaps in its features that I find. Eclipse or Netbeans are great Java IDEs but I wouldn't use them for C++, right tool for the job and all that (although I hear CDT is improving).

---

### Post by forrestcupp on 2009-02-17
> **Kazade said:**
>

The only other IDE I've found that comes close in code completion was MingGW Studio, and you can't even hardly find that anymore.  It's like it vanished from the earth.

I will say that Code::Blocks' implementation of code completion is better than DevCpp's, though.

### Post by LowSky on 2009-02-17
Good things

They brought stability to the computer industry. Computer Software and even computer components makers didn't have to worry about what type of OS will be running the computer, less work designing drivers. Before Windows gained popularity there wer like 5 different choices for operating systems, two that come to mind are Apples MacIntosh and IBM OS/2

People could buy a computer based on specification rather than usefullnes.
For example why buy this machine when this one is faster, and the clerk could prove it. This also made Intel a household name.

Windows95 install CD had a free music video of Weezer playing the song "Buddy Holly"

Without prebundling Internet Explorer, people may still be paying companies like AOL (remember how back then you had to open the AOL program to sign on and search web pages)

Without Windows the term Blue Screen of Death wouldn't be ever heard.

> Have you tried a recent Code::Blocks nightly? I've recently had to work on a project which required me to use VC++ express edition (I don't know whether Intellisense is better in the full version) for long periods of time but I was continually frustrated with Intellisense just not working. I was relieved to get back to the more reliable code completion in Code::Blocks. As I said, I haven't tried the full version of VC++ so it may perform better.

Yeah, I was using the nightly builds of Code::Blocks, and I still didn't like it as well.  But I will say that if I couldn't use MS Visual Studio, that would definitely be the way to go.  I liked it better than DevCpp. 

Intellisense is the same in all versions of VC++.  It *is* kind of glitchy, but there are ways of dealing with the glitches, and when it works, it works really good.  If Intellisense isn't working, you usually just have to save your entire project, and it will start working.  If that doesn't help, the guaranteed way of getting it to work is to go to your project's folder and delete the .ncb file.  Then it will regenerate Intellisense for that project and start working.  It usually messes up because either you haven't saved your project yet for the first time, or you've changed some included headers without saving your project.  Also, if you're using some framework, like wxWidgets, sometimes you have to set it up to work with that framework.

But when Intellisense works, I maintain that nothing else even comes close.

---

### Post by Kazade on 2009-02-21

Windows 95 didn't need to be that revolutionary OS (and by the point of Win95 MS already had the market share and momentum). OS/2 was invented by Microsoft and IBM, Bill Gates said the following about it:
> 
I believe OS/2 is destined to be the most important operating system, and possibly program, of all time. 


That was in 1987, then he used what was learned during the OS/2 partnership to come up with Windows. If he didn't do that, then OS/2 would have been the popular desktop and we likely would have seen a whole different tree of events play out with IBM having majority control of it. (Although OS/2 was mainly designed to push IBM hardware but I still believe that if Windows didn't get there first, someone else would have done soon after).

As I said, right place, right time. There was all sorts of other stuff going on at the time, Apple was innovating then as well, Microsoft got lucky and they've been riding that wave ever since. They are like an army that accidentally found themselves at the top of a hill before a battle.

Finally, I am not a gamer and don't like the virtual gaming industry, thus the shift was easier for me than most people here in my country. There are plenty of alternative applications in Linux to the MS OS softwares and I never had a problem learning them because they are so similar.

### Post by Faolan84 on 2009-02-21
well they didn't have to sacrifice speed for aesthetics. The fact is that Vista is based on a bloated, aging code base that needs to be put to rest alongside such things as the emo style fad, the policies of George W Bush, the ability to use cell phones in the cinema and in restaurants, and any format Sony has every produced in attempt to lock up the market.

Honestly I don't think vista looks all that great, and that one thing I do like about KDE 4.x over Vista in terms of style: choice. Honestly, I prefer more ambient and neutral colors as opposed to some slick artificial plastic look. Glass, certain types of metal, and wood actually have nice textures that I think one could base a pleasing style on. But that is just my tastes and opinions.

However, the developers could make Vista look as plain as newly plowed Nebraska corn field and the fact is that it would still be slow. It's not what is on top that is slowing Vista down inasmuch as what is running underneath it. Windows NT itself was a great concept -- but it was never really realized. The greatest parts of what was to be their next generation OS never came to fruition and that is the greatest tragedy of Microsoft: how they made off with billions based on false promises and lies to their customer base.

Windows NT was in 1993 to become eventually what was know as Cairo which in many ways resembles in many ways what Linux is today. The ultimate goal of the NT project was to have a core OS that could run a variety of different binary compilations. The idea was that the execution of a program would be handled by a "subsystem" which could be Windows 3.x, Windows 9x, Windows NT, MS-DOS, BSD, POSIX, and a couple other systems. However, by default only the 3.x, 9x, NT, and DOS subsystems are truly built-in components that can be ran without alteration while the BSD, POSIX, and other "subsystems" are compile-time and only exist as a formality that allows code produced on a UNIX-type system to be compiled on an NT system and then essentially converted trough the process of compilation to run under the NT subsystem. In effect, that means the non-Windows and non-Microsoft subsystem layers are not truly subsystems in the same sense as the others.

Cairo on the other was to be the future of NT: a modular OS that had a sh-like shell (called Monad in the recent projects) and a special database like indexing file system that was unlike NTFS and closer to something like a compromise of ZFS and BeFS in terms of actual functionality from an end users perspective. In other words, a magical happlyland file system.

All of these things more than likely were never really seriously developed and were probably just a way for Microsoft to announced to their early client base that the next release was going to be so much better, but only if they have their clients support (that is, money). The fact is that even with Windows 7 they are only part of the way to the Cairo vision they announced in the early 1990s. The fact is, had they not have made these promises and had they not have threatened vendors with the illegal price discrimination tactics they would not have survived. Microsoft has far to long rode on the success and advancements of Sun, Apple, Amiga, NeXT, BeOS, IBM, and many other companies that tried their hand in the market only to get "embraced, extended," and screwed by them.

Vista is the product of the Microsoft corporate mono-culture that they have themselves created. Their ability to create even a decent operating system is diminished by the fact that almost everything they have ever "innovated" has been ripped from somewhere else. They only contenders offering anything creative is off-market open source projects and their small corporate backers who have very little in the way of capital and the hipster Apple which is looked at by other corporate industries like crazy a crazy teenager at a rave -- you know the one with the thick rimmed glasses and the black and red cashmere wardrobe with the black mascara and died hair.

In the end the only real threat to Microsoft is that small community of open source and Microsoft has spent billions of dollars to make that community of developers like like charlatans, thieves, pirates, and outright liars. All this while the quality of their products as of late has declined. Maybe some of that money put into lying about the legal troubles companies running Linux could run into, maybe they should have used those funds to actually develop their products and deliver to their clients -- both present and future -- something worth paying for. Instead they've wasted many billions and millions on things like SCO to threaten vendors and Fortune 500 companies from using their competition.

It would be best if Microsoft just sticks with software and video games and not OS development. MS Office isn't really all that bad, though it's cluttered with excess features no one really uses. On top of that Age of Empires, Halo, Thrones and Patriots have all been great games. In addition they did a fairly good job with the X-Box albeit they probably should have focused a bit more on product quality with the hardware.

---

Screw it, I am going to learn this unix stuff if it kills me.

### Post by Wollombi on 2009-02-22
> **Dragonbite said:**
> To be honest, I do not like all of Microsoft's business practices but a lot of this bickering sounds like standard capitalism with the only difference being Microsoft has done it very well.

I hear you there.  I'm for capitalism, so long as it's done ethically.  What I mean is that you don't license someone's stuff, then bastardize it on your monopoly OS so that it doesn't work right (anyone remember this with Java?).  Indeed, MS is mostly just a capitalist entity, but that entity has also shown a shocking lack of ethics, and this is equally important.  Do I hate them for making money?  Absolutely not.  Do I hate some of their less than honest practices.  Yes, I do.  

MS's lack of long term vision in exchange for short term gains has effectively hamstrung them today.

See, there is no clear yes or no on the matter in many regards.  In one sense MS deserves credit for:

- making computers easy to use and enjoy for Joe six-pack, or those who don't want/can't take the time to learn what may be a more robust, but also more complicated, system.

- standardizing an interface that everyone is familiar with (due to their market dominance, but that is beside the point), so that users can sit down to nearly any PC and know how to use it/where things are.

- Microsoft Office - yes, it has flaws, but nearly all office suites do.

- Some pretty decent programming languages/interfaces.

- Business acumen.  Hey, even when they are doing it above board, they're shrewd buggers.  They've managed to dominate a market with a product that honestly isn't all that innovative, if at all, by providing/highlighting things that the mainstream wanted.



OTOH, MS deserves just criticism for:

- Making "computer security" a viable business.

- Destroying overall industry quality standards with the release then patch paradigm.

- FUD.  Let's face it - MS has spread, and continues to spread, some pretty bald-faced lies about anything it perceives as a competitor.  Open Source projects have constantly had to battle this "open source is insecure" BS from MS.

- Abusing monopoly power.  Not only did they lose the U.S. court case (although only getting the lightest wrist-slap), they are continually getting hammered in other parts of the world (EU, Asia, etc.).  This seems a hard lesson for them to learn.

- Flat out nastiness - bastardizing Java is just one example, but the plans were/are laid for open standards to be treated the same.  Remember the Halloween Memo?  Sheer hubris, and in some cases illegal (Sun successfully sued over the Java debacle, which is why Java VM's aren't bundled into Windows products any longer).


The way I see it, MS isn't hated blindly.  They have in many cases created a great deal of the ill will that is directed toward them.  You reap what you sow.  Vista has only helped people to look for alternatives (Windows is now below 90% market share for the first time since achieving market dominance).  I don't hate all things Microsoft, but I have come to hate the price tag (not $) that comes with using their products (unplugged security holes, pre-programmed back doors - google "Microsoft and the NSA", and, with Vista anyway, the arrogance that they feel they can determine what hardware I can use on MY computer - see here: [http://www.cs.auckland.ac.nz/~pgut001/pubs/vista_cost.txt](http://www.cs.auckland.ac.nz/~pgut001/pubs/vista_cost.txt)).  Vista especially led me to take a look a Linux for the first time in nearly a decade, and you know what?  I liked it.  I haven't looked back.

My use of Linux isn't due to blind MS hatred, it's out of preference for Linux and the freedom it has afforded me.  I still have a Windows partition for games and the odd app that won't run in WINE, but I almost never use it (with the exception of games).

As far as making money, more power to them, so long as it's done above-board.

---

### Post by toupeiro on 2009-02-24
I think Microsoft has done some great things for the business world, but a lot of people here give them too much credit.  They are not some desktop GUI pioneering company that set the standard.  They've begged, borrowed, bought, and in many cases outright stolen from the likes of Apple and IBM to make a UI worth a damn.  Anyone using computers long enough to have used OS/2 Warp knows exactly where the "revolutionary" windows startmenu was derrived from.  Everything today is an evolution of that, which was really a concept that belongs to IBM in their partnership over that OS.

Active Directory is wonderful!  Quite possibly the best implementation of a directory model I've ever worked with.  Still, they didn't engineer this themselves.  Active Directory is HEAVILY modeled after Novells NDS (netware 4+) and fundamentally based on LDAP.  Microsoft MMC, which was (and in their GUI, it still is) the core framework of ALL of their system management tools is a blatent ripoff of Novell's ConsoleOne which pre-dates MMC by quite a bit..  I don't discredit the great backoffice tools they developed, but their methodologies they are bringing to the Microsoft computing world were already brought by third parties and used quite emmensely in the business world long before Microsoft did it.

If you strip the buy-outs, hostile takeovers and malpractice out of the last 15 years of MS offerings, they actually have a very small baseline of offerings that are purely Microsoft developed and deployed.  Forgive me if I am not pouring over myself at their display of innovation.  What they do, they do good, but they stand on the shoulders of real innovation, rebranding it as their own at any cost or price and that doesn't make a great company, just a greedy one.


Microsoft Sync, however, thats golden!  I think Microsoft should get out of the OS business and make vehicle NAV systems...:p

Without question, nobody can take what they've accomplished in shear market dominance and visibility away from them, and nobody can deny that likely for quite some time still to come, Microsoft will remain the dominant OS on IBM/PC's.  To me, the company has lost a sense of what it means to have customers, and to deliver quality that customers really want.  Windows is now something so huge that they cannot truly meet the demands of their business customers or their residential customers, and there is nobody left really to steal from that you can claim yours because the biggest competition windows has right now is absolutely free and protected as such.  For the first time, in this the vista generation (6) of Windows, they have to come up with their own ideas.  I think the world has been seeing just how innovative they are without someone to buy up.... 

I've said it many times.  There is still, and will be for a long time I am sure, a place for windows, and I am sure I will still be supporting it for years to come, but on its current path it will never have a place on my own systems again.

### Post by boombox1387 on 2009-02-27
Several things I like have already been mentioned, but I feel like making my own list of things I like:

-Visual Studio is a really good IDE.  I know this has been mentioned several times, but I really do like working with Visual Studio and I don't even do much programming.

-Updates!  They happen in the background without my input.  I don't have to give an administrative password every time.  I can trust that updating my computer won't give me a graphics driver that puts my monitor to sleep every time my computer starts up.

-Software that I use with Windows seems more stable (less beta-like) than the equivalent software I use with Ubuntu.  Applications crash less frequently.  MS doesn't exactly deserve the credit for this, just an observation.

But in the end I use Ubuntu almost exclusively, so there must be some good reason for it :)

### Post by open_coder on 2009-03-05
[QUOTE=jespdj]Microsoft is not so good at innovation. What they are good at is taking technology invented by others, building their own version and then adding proprietary stuff to it, and making everybody use their proprietary version. That's how Microsoft corrupts open standards. An example where they tried this and failed is with Sun's Java. Sun sued Microsoft and Microsoft lost. After that they made the .NET framework which is built on exactly the same concepts as Java EE. The .NET framework is not bad, but Microsoft didn't invent the concepts on which it is built.[/QUOTE]

The real innovation in .NET is not the vm, but the libraries that enable programmers from multiple backgrounds to work on the same project. I do love Visual Studio as an IDE, but i think .NET needs some work.

[QUOTE=jflaker]
many have made comments on their great keyboards, mice and joystick.......Most of those devices are manufactured by Logitec and stamped with Microsoft's logo...[/QUOTE]

Um... What? I can believe that they outsource their manufacturing, but i doubt its to Logitec. I really need to see proof of that.

The problem with the Microsoft versus Linux conversation is that people will never change their minds. I used Windows throughout school and installed Linux as a hobby when I was in 10th grade after I built my first computer. I used to dual boot with Windows so I could play games and do school work. I eventually fell in love with being able to tweak the system how I needed. Eventually I booted only Linux, and now I run Vista and the Windows 7 Beta in VirtualBox.

The problem is that GNU zealots hurt the Linux Desktop market more than they help. I think that Microsoft and Apple have done a lot for the computer industry. However, I don't think you can make an argument that without them this would have been done by another company. There is no way to know that for certain, because it didn't happen that way.

Competition is good. I don't hate Microsoft. In fact, I really love the work they doing on Windows 7. The new taskbar is basically the taskbar I've wanted for years. I think that Office 2007 is a great suite of tools. Visual Studio is pretty good IDE. And even though I have always been a Playstation guy, when I saw the New Xbox Experience, I envied the Xbox users. 

All this anti-Microsoft sentiment really hurts us as a community. It's okay to not like Microsoft, but when I see "Micro$oft" or "m$" on a forum, I die a little inside, because I know that reflects on me as a member of the Linux community. Those kinds of things make us look childish. It's time to grow out of that.

### Post by victor7 on 2009-03-08
Wow!  Great reading late at night.  I've been 'into' computers for awhile, writing my first program to find square roots correct to 5 decimal places.  Took 2 months to get it working.  Used Hollerith cards and '0' and '1'.  Worked my way up to current vista64 on a homebuilt system.  I own some MS stock, and so have a vested interest in having it succeed.  

I'm a committed capitalist;  if someone has something someone else wants to buy, that's great.  There's no reason that anything in the capitalist system should be 'free' for anyone to take it.  what I like about Microsoft is that whatever I need a computer for, I have available, whether from MS or another provider smart enough to create something I'm willing to pay for.

I'm having more problems with MS recently in their increasing habit of including more in the os that I want or need.  I estimate that vista64 has about 50% of inclusives that are useless to me, but I can't get rid of them.  I am growing to hate it when anyone tries to take over my entire system just because I bought one issue of their program.  Symantic leaps immediately to mind.

Most of my problems, though, are not with MS nearly so much as they are caused by people that either hate MS and try to destroy it, making me 'collateral damage', or who just need to destroy anything they can because it's easier to destroy than to create.

I'm coming back around to Linux now, as I have time and interest in working to increase the utility and beauty of the shareware approach.  I'm not doing it to make money, or to save money, or to make money for someone else.  It's fun and stimulating and psychologically invigorating.

If its going to involve money, though, I'll probably us MS related stuff because most people I associate with use MS.  Also, I'm an original MYST player ('94) and recent MOUL player.  I also fly, and Microsoft Simulator is really well done in that regard.  XBOX for Tiger Woods Golf, and with the other things being developed in that area, are also exciting.

Bottom line, there are a lot of people that don't know me, and don't lose out by not knowing me.  Maybe they're even better off.  I suspect the same is true of both Microsoft and Linux.

### Post by miegiel on 2009-03-08
> **drascus said:**
> Of course Microsoft does some positive things. I mean it goes without saying that even a bad person does good things now and then even if totally by accident.

Good point, there goes my "microsoft brings color to my life" and "they make good hardware" phun. But it made me think of the good things microsoft has "accidentally" done

Microsoft is actually great at one thing, though it's not all good. How could it be? It's microsoft! I don't know if the idea is originally theirs, they might have stolen it. Or - who knows - the idea might might have come out of the brain of mr Gates himself.

In short the idea is: "When you've done half work, you're 80% done, that's kind of finished"

Compared to the time and hands needed to fully complete a piece of software, you only need half that time to get it 80% functional and not so buggy people that will be demanding their money back. This saves 50% in costs initially and you can release the application twice as soon. On the down side support costs will be higher (patches, more users phoning help desks). But still you gain more then you loose, whether you make and sell or buy and use the software. As far as I know microsoft is the first company to build their business on this principle and in the process motoring the revolution in home computing.

Now microsoft only needs a marketing machine to cover up the fact that they're doing the world a great service by being the cheap shitty software company they are and convince the world their latest product is greater than what their competitor released a year ago. And this is where the trouble starts. When the marketing is done, everyone expects top dollar for half price and people get disappointed because it's only 80% of top dollar for half price.

### Post by Robocoastie on 2009-03-26
> **Dragonbite said:**
> Having an opinion for something, like Linux, is not difficult and it is easy to then demonize and put down the "competition", like Microsoft, at every turn.

Unfortunately this is also part of the criteria of a fanboy, or fanatic.

The question is, can you say something positive aboue Microsoft? Can you actually "see" from the other person's point of view?

Since there are so many arguments both for- and against-Microsoft there has to be some merit to both sides of the coin, but can you find it?

I've noticed a growing anti-Microsoft feeling but I wonder if it is blind following the herd like sheep or are you actually making a choice?

I'll bite. Something positive: Win 7 beta is actually a good product (from a home user standpoint). It's "library" feature is very handy as well as its much simplified home networking. The inclusion of the newer codecs such as AAC, mp4 formats/h26x, save/print as xps and others are a nice upgrade. And as others have said, it has gaming market cornered, though that is starting to be a moot point due to the powerful consoles we have now. I still hear from multimedia professionals that they prefer MAC still although I've yet to hear a valid argument for why the Windows versions don't work as well.

Linux: still sucks at multimedia. If it/we ever want to see a viable commercial Linux computer market then we have GOT to let the vendors include prop. codecs. Admit it people, the first thing you install on your linux rigs is the MSFT/Apple/Real/Flash/Java/etc... codecs and programs isn't it? Some web sites are still touch and go in Linux even with the proprietory programs installed. My college web site for example has days the java applets load right up and times it spins and spins and never does. Times like that I NEED it to work, so I have Vista Basic running in VirtuaBox for that.

This may look like I'm admitting that Windows wins but it doesn't for a very crucial reason: stability. Windows (both vista basic and Win7 beta) run faster and better in VirtualBox than they do natively. I rarely get the windows lockups under VB that I do natively and if it does the whole system doesn't go down. Then there's the whole security thing.

What would be great is either a) the web used open standards more or b) Apple gave up being so hardware proprietary and opened up to third party vendors and us do it yourselfers.

### Post by k2t0f12d on 2009-03-31
Other developer's programs that have been implemented on Windows' API is not something that Windows does better.  Any operating system is going to have an API for which applications may be written.  Most programs can be easily adapted for other platforms if source is available.  There are many programs that distribute source that is written preconfigured to be built on every major platform.  Even the API's themselves can be ported, eg. *[COLOR="DarkRed"]**WINE**[/COLOR]*.  Because Micro$oft has incomplete documentation of their API, and also do not redistribute source, it is impossible for a developer to ascertain that they have done the best implementation of their program.  The GNU and Linux platform may also have less then perfect documentation; however, source is available with which anyone may educate themselves for best practices.  With Windows, the *real* benefit is the opportunity to extract a monetary ransom by offering binary copies of a program with licensing fees to the captive audience of a large install base that was gained through illegal anti-competitive actions (which is the only thing that Micro$oft actually does do better then anyone else).

### Post by ndefontenay on 2009-04-28
I think it was about Microsoft and not windows.

Microsoft is huge. They provide solutions such as hospital software, Business intelligence, data mining, ETL with SSSIS, SQL Server 2008 with pretty advance replication.

For all of these there's an alternative. Oracle is much better than SQL Server when it gets to the big numbers and its replication is not to par with oracle RAC. 

Where microsoft gets its strength is that all of these actually talks to each others. None of its tools are great, but together they are deadly. 

When microsoft goes to the owner of a company looking for a solution, specially if that company doesn't have great consultancy because of its size. They tell them, you won't have a problem it all talks together because it's all one brand.

This makes sense. This is something that the non technical person relates too. one umbrella, one policy, one direction. Yes! It must be possible!

Then, when you got all that, it just makes sense to go to windows on the desktop. And when you got windows at the office, you have windows at home. Because... You want to make sure it talks.

That's how the average user think.

Also, microsoft doesn't work only at software. They have an extensive training program which also rubs CTOs the right way. These guys will consider: "I won't have any problem finding staff which knows about it and since they are many, they will be cheaper too".

This is all the positive about microsoft.

I got MAC and Linux at home though.

### Post by Ticketoride on 2009-04-28
I don't get distracted by Politicking, I use whatever Operating System, Applications and Hardware that gets the Job done in the smoothest, fastest and most efficient Fashion, with the least Amount of futzing or Troubleshooting possible.

I don't care if Bill Gates doubles his Income next Year, Microsoft plays Gestapo with its Vendors, or gets sued for $ 100 Billion next Friday. I am the End User, and I am only interested in what works best, and at this Point MS & Friends have all the Toys & Apps I like to use.

You can't compare Open Office with MS Office, or Photoshop with Gimp. Granted if you just want to type a Letter to Mommy, or crop a JPG then its all the same. Office '95 & Photoshop 3.x of the Mid-90's still have the Edge on either. Linux is coming up the Line, and hopefully will come out near the top one Day. 

But all this MS Bashing and how superior Linux is deserves little more than a Chuckle. Its just cute Fanaticism/Loyalties in a little Corner on the Net, and hardly taken seriously anywhere else. That's like the Gung-Ho "FIAT Auto Club" saber-rattling the General Motors Boys ...

I can use both Linux and Windows, without having to take any Sides. There are no Sides, and why should anyone care whether Linux becomes more popular than MS unless politics and/or Money are behind it?

Fair Labour/Business Practices, the Starving in Africa, Responsible Gov't are all worthy of Support, Petition or Protest one Way or the other, but an Operating System? Give me a Break ... :lol:

### Post by paddydd on 2009-05-10
So let's see it from the other side?

Hmmm. Good Apps -- but they would be just as good maybe even better running on Linux.

They work on hardware -- but then again they are pre-loaded. If Linux was pre-loaded then regular folks have no big install troubles --> think System76.

OS and the stuff underneath the apps - downright scary. Security? Stability? Ability to truly multi-task? Now I don't hate the people at MS nor all it's supporters-process? How scary is it that IE has security holes in it that you can drive a truck through? How scary is it that my corporate provided computer with a IT supported MS install lives by the credo -- re-boot once a week whether you like it or not and at various points had viruses on it that the Norton could not find. How scary is it that my non-technical sister clicked one to many times and loaded up a virus that not only could not be found but one that actively attacked the programs loaded to destroy it.

**So it sounds to me like a Faustian Bargin... **You know the one where the devil says try this you'll like it --> Get good apps... and pray no one breaks in. In the end you will not like the bargain.

**Now I don't hate the people at MS nor all it's supporters** and let's face it they employ lots of folks. So I don't want to see them go away. But on the other hand the OS they provide is a monopoly even with Linux and OSX. How many companies use Linux or OSX internally?

Think of it this way...If there was one company that ran the electrical grid or the water supply in the entire US and had the reliability and security of MS then the US Government would come in and separate the apps from the OS and make the OS a utility like it should be. They would regulate it and likely create a chartered company that could stabilize and lock down windows OS or perhaps do away with it and replace it with a UNIX variant like Apple Did. 

I'm not some drone that lives in a herd like the author of this thread thinks. I don't think many people in UBUNTU are because let's face it.. if you came to the conclusion that you want out from Windows it's NOT because the GUI is nice or the apps don't work. It's because of the system and you arrived at the conclusion like I did through a bunch of BS experience.

Paddy

Paying, paying, paying, and never getting more than a promise that *if* I get my system finally built up with the software, hardware, service packs, anti-virus that actually does a decent job, etc.. etc.. that right about then, out comes a *New and Improved* version of Windows, and I get to start all over again. Then to put the icing on the cake, if I should have the audacity to build or buy a new computer, I have to ask permission to transport the OS I payed all that money for. 

Nah, its as bad as its being painted, call me a fanboy, zealot, or any other adjective that allows one person to put another person in a box; but I do have strong reasons for my disdain for MS products, not the least of which are the reasons already mentioned; I would add, I also make great objection to the monopolistic business practices, as do several governments of the planet.

sudo /etc/init.d/rant --OFF

### Post by GOfree on 2009-05-19
You can argue all you want that MS makes good software. Maybe they have had a success here or there, but we wouldn't be here is we didn't believe that we can expect more -- realizing that people can make their own software and do not need Father Corporation to do everything for us.

Did Microsoft really advance the cause of home computers? I don't think so. I think that they set the cause back by at least a decade. They got a strangle-hold on the technology and not only did everything in their power to prevent innovation from outside, but actually convinced much of the world that we *should* be dependent on them.

Was it power that corrupted Microsoft? No, Bill Gates was a greedy tyrant from the very beginning. He attacked the free software community over BASIC for crissakes -- tried to destroy computer hobbyism -- the very idea that real people can and should learn to use their computers for themselves (and their neighbours, Ubuntu). It has been the whole history of that company to oppress the free spirit of the people.

I am sorry, you asked what good their is to say about Microsoft... I think their name has a funny phallic meaning.

No, seriously, would you ask people, out of fairness, to try to see the good in <cut> or William Randolph Hearst? I should hope not. These people are/were tyrants, their empires that they created were/are destructive to the ideals of democracy, and deserve to be dismantled. An Autobahn or an MS Office do not redeem the tyrants for what they do to stifle the growth of the human spirit.

These people and their empires deserve the ire of the people. They should be held up as examples for the people so that the people may see their captors and learn to recognize them, and as warnings to others who would try to be like them.

Tyrannicide is a healthy human impulse. 

Fight the Power!

Free Software Now!

---

### Post by GOfree on 2009-05-19
Okay, obviously I have an ideological commitment here...

Let me apologize... I don't want to insult or offend anyone, but I am passionate about the potential of Free Software and have a hard time with people asking (or "challenging") me to be sympathetic with someone (or something) that I consider an oppressor (or oppressive). 

Bill Gates and Microsoft have been against the ideals of Free Software from Day One. Why should we be sympathetic to them?

Anyway...

The argument by the supposed quality of MS products does not wash with me and many others.

> 
"If your software would keep us divided and helpless, please don't write it. We are better off without it. We will find other ways to use our computers, and preserve our freedom."

### Post by FirstByté on 2009-05-26
My thoughts...

**Windoz:**

Startup- 
[LIST]
[/LIST]Very brilliant startup with impressive [32bit splash animation--Vista]

Sometimes I wonder if my Juanty or earlier distros run on 32bits. Virtualize XP seamless-mode, and you'll see a bit of diff in the black rendering. 

Yes we are the "blends of Brown themed guys" and proud of it... but maybe our hue can be improved a bit more.

*Standardized* Dev Apps-

I ran Visual Studio faithfully till the .Net pain... my days with NetBeans explained to me I miss VS6 in a way.

Take it or leave it. VS6 is one of the major success to BILLION apps on Windoz... it's easy to use and DOCUMENTATION is widely available.
OSI, PKI, DES et al., all have standards I look forward to a day apps on linux would have standardization and plainly easy-to-use/deploy Dev Apps such as VS... and fast too ;)
No wonder MS invested greatly on their VS... young minds and new minds keep trying out their creativity with it.

Imaging Apps-
MS Picture Manager is good and simple, PhotoShop is a bit ahead of GIMP {IMO}, which has scattered UI windows. Thanks to the Blender guys, some 3D animators can still try out our talents that once blossom.

Desktop Publishing Apps- 
MS Office 2007, way too much Office. OpenOffice is great, but MS Office2007 is in a planet of it's own. I have to Virtualize just to squeeze it's juice.

Games-
My little years devotion to DirectX programming tells me, Windoz would stay ahead of the games porting platforms for a while. OpenGL is great but ... I know little about OpenGL.

Shutting Down-
Brilliant FadeOut and ease-out.

Hardware:
Though OEM based... no hustles with Fingerprint Readers, Remote Controllers, headphone. Sorry, Ubuntu has a brilliant battery charging management. Wanna confirm, wait till your system is charged full and unplug the charger from source. On Windoz the LED on the charger [if you have] dims off immediately, on Ubuntu... it stays longer.. meaning... it wasnt charging ... otherwise runing directly

**Ubuntu**
Great Kudos to the devoted developers. We are here today ALL because you made the sacrifice. :popcorn:

StartUp-
I miss the Intrepid splash screen/boot screen.  The splash was way too Ubuntu.
The Jaunty's logon screen is very colourful.

Colour Depth-
A little more to do with blending in [anti-aliasing] and font relaxing.

Shutting Down-
Way too sudden with little or no ease-outs. The new 60secs notification is much appreciated :guitar:

Apps:

Standardize apps development or at least a great VISUAL Dev IDE then, easily, my colleagues and I would not sleep till we see Ubuntu living its dream--Linux for humans. More home-related utilities... and the future is sure. 

**Bottom line:** Windoz has done a great job. Buggy Vista and a pot full of viruses easily pushed to the more reliable side--Linux.:)

> **Dragonbite said:**
> Microsoft Publisher is much more intuitive and easy to use than Scribus.

Publisher is designed as a way for amateurs to make newsletters and birthday cards. Scribus is meant to be more of a professional typesetting program. In particular, Scribus' interface emulates QuarkXPress which was THE industry standard for many years (and is still widely used).

No real typesetter uses Microsoft Publisher, except to export customers' Publisher documents into a format he/she can actually use. You won't find them using Scribus either, but that's partly because it's missing the featureset of programs like Indesign, and partly because Scribus isn't well-known.

So, you can't really compare the two.

---

### Post by rookcifer on 2009-05-28
> **Dragonbite said:**
> Having an opinion for something, like Linux, is not difficult and it is easy to then demonize and put down the "competition", like Microsoft, at every turn.

Unfortunately this is also part of the criteria of a fanboy, or fanatic.

The question is, can you say something positive aboue Microsoft? Can you actually "see" from the other person's point of view?

Since there are so many arguments both for- and against-Microsoft there has to be some merit to both sides of the coin, but can you find it?

I've noticed a growing anti-Microsoft feeling but I wonder if it is blind following the herd like sheep or are you actually making a choice?

In all honesty I see no advantage to Microsoft's Windows when compared to Linux.  I have used Windows since 95 (and then 98, ME, XP) before I began using Linux full time a couple years ago.

The only advantages (or disadvantage really) I can see in Windows is that many companies, businesses, hospitals, etc. are locked into the Windows platform due to some of the software they run.  They often spend a lot of money for this proprietary, specialized software and just cannot afford to go open-source.  So, this is one reason to use Windows.

Another advantage might be AutoCAD.  Linux still does not have a viable alternative (though there are several projects in the works that will change this).  But, how many people are engineers or architects?  Not many.

The last advantage to Windows would be PC gaming.  Sure, some games will run under WINE, but many wont.  This is not a weakness of Linux, though.  It's just that the game companies don't port their games to Linux (small market-share).

Basically, just about all possible "advantages" to Windows are not a weakness with Linux, but rather are "third party" developer problems. People become locked into Windows due to some software (M$ likes it this way and has spent years on making people dependent on their monopoly). Some software just hasn't made it to Linux yet.  But this will change.

---

### Post by k2t0f12d on 2009-05-28
> **Shibblet said:**
> A better idea would be for Microsoft to fully support Wine.  They could call it a community donation, and write it off...  ;)

Then MS could continue to sell software, to Linux (Ubuntu) users.I hoped I hadn't needed to mention that this doesn't make sense. WINE *is* the software Micro$oft wants to sell. WINE *does* the same job that Micro$oft's flagship software does. If anything, Micro$oft will want to take any applications and games whose development it funds and ensure they runs on its own and *only* its own platform(s). This will drives Windows - their bread and butter. There are millions of GNU+Linux users, *maybe*, and *billions* of Windows users. Micro$oft would not make it any easier for that to change to billions of GNU+Linux users and millions of Windows users by supporting a competing platform in order to sell us a few licenses.

### Post by theDaveTheRave on 2009-06-09
I probably come at this from a different angle to most people, as I have never had a pc that had windows on it.... well I did for about 2 months in 1996 (ish).

What I think really scares people off of open source.... the lines in the EULA that say "this software comes with absolutely no warranty".

Maybe it should come with a warranty, but expressly say to visit a specified site for the systems that function with it.

The truth is that whatever people buy goes wrong, how many times have you had to "re-install" XP, Vista, Ubuntu??

I'm sure that there is scope for companies to "sell" support for linux, in the same way that you can get support for "windows".

The issue here is that Linux doesn't have the scale that windows does, and hence thing like being a "certified red hat professional" doesn't have the same authority as a "certified microsoft professional".

As others have said, if windows wasn't installed by the manufacturers it probably wouldn't be as popular as it it!

What surprises me about PCs is the following, everyone always complains that there computer is "getting old and slow". So they go and get a completely new system rather than investigate why it is slow?

That is where microsoft have won the game, convincing people that the new version of windows is so much better than the old one.

With linux there seems to be more of a "if your computer is going slow, tweak it" mentality, "put on a different windowing system", "remove the eyecandy", "make your computer go faster with openbox (or whatever else)".

Until the various distro's get out of this idea and SELL the sytem as a "WOW" look at this, on a new desktop, people aren't going to take notice.

We need to compare like with like. Run benchmark testes on a clean install of Vista and then do a clean install on the same hardware with various forms of linux.

We will never sell linux on the principle of "you get less malware", "it has a smaller system resources footprint" unless you show it.

The netbooks first came about due to the desire of a few people to create an open system resource for "donating" cheap pc's into the classroom in "third world" countries in the One Laptop Per Child as an educational tool. This has seemingly worked, but typically as soon as microsoft thought they may be loosing out they donated an "trimmed down" version of XP for people to use.

those microsoft marketing people aren't silly ;)

David

### Post by zipperback on 2009-06-16
> **Dragonbite said:**
> 
I've noticed a growing anti-Microsoft feeling but I wonder if it is blind following the herd like sheep or are you actually making a choice?

We (MY family and I) run Ubuntu Linux because we have made the choice to do so, and we enjoy the freedoms that such a choice allows us. Nonrestrictive licenses, Thousand of applications available from the repositories which are available for installation with a few mouse clicks, Rapid community response times when needing a solution to a problem which might be encountered, and the ability to actually access the source code for the operating system and various applications should I wish to view it, modify it, or whatever else I might want to do with it. And I have on a number of occasions made modifications to open source applications to enable them to meet my own personal needs. The same cannot be said when dealing with a closed source application.

In addition, when I purchased my most recent laptop, an Acer Apire 5050-3371 64bit system, it came preinstalled with Windows Vista which ran OK in it's preinstalled stated by itself, however, quite a bit of software which I hade legally purchased over the years would NOT run ok under Vista on my laptop, although those same applications would run without any major issues using WINE with Linux. 

Since moving 100% to Linux, I no longer care about problems with Windows, and I am quite happy to let those people who remain loyal to Microsoft Windows to stay exactly where they are, spending unreasonable amounts of money on their operating system, having to buy expensive applications (example: MS Office in lieu of OpenOffice.org), and having to deal with their constant need to protect themselves against the nearly daily discovery of backdoors, malware, and virus infections.

Just some of my thoughts on the subject...

- zipperback
:popcorn:

i dont support huge monopolies like microsoft (just a personal thing though)

most computer companies are forced to use windows on their products (thank you system76 and even apple for fighting this :))

in windows, you run the computer under root (unless you're using a guest/limited account), yet again another security hole. linux (for the most part) and apple dont follow this practice

customization in windows is limited in most cases and sometimes non-existant (as in chaning from the aero interface to something else in vista, only exception to this that i can think of is windowsblinds for xp, but im referring to default features of the OS instead of 3rd party)

---

i dont hate microsoft and windows because of these faults though (in fact, im running 2 computers with vista and xp), but becuase of all the bad things about windows, i like using linux much more. only reason i keep windows really is for games and TI Connect

### Post by Dragonbite on 2009-07-07
Ok, just thought of something else that Microsoft does pretty well.

Normally I use [http://maps.google.com]("http://maps.google.com") to get directions and all. When I am looking at a piece of property, though, I love using [http://maps.live.com]("http://maps.live.com")'s "Birds Eye View" which gives you a picture view from just a couple hundred feet altitude instead of satelite, plus you can rotate and veiw it on 4 sides.

So when my wife and I are looking at a house (foreclosure, what friends just bought, etc.) we can get a pretty good view of the house, the neighborhood and the lot from multiple angles.  Try it sometime!
