### Post by privatejarhead on 2009-07-29
because Microsoft has become the dominate OS in the computing world, computer companies need to standardize their hardware so it all works. I see that as amazing and a very good thing in general.

because every release of a new Microsoft OS takes up more and more of a computer's resources, computers need more and more RAM and hard drive space to run the dominate OS. since Vista needs 2GB to run smoothly (for example), most computers today have at least 2-3GB installed. Today, one could pick up a decent Vista computer anywhere from $400-$600, but if you wanted 2-3GB RAM 5 or more years ago, it would have cost an arm and a leg. case in point, microsoft's OSes indirectly affect the price of computer hardware so that it gets cheaper and cheaper to own better machines.

it's nice that gates donates a sizable amount of money

computers with windows are some of the cheapest machines out there

microsoft's relations with the eu and the doj are always fun to look into from time to time :lolflag:

xp was amazing

i <3 halo and the xbox 360

loads of 3rd-party apps for windows (and mac i suppose). linux really needs to work on this if it hopes to have at least the same amount of marketshare as apple (5-8%)

my TI connection software only works with windows, so thats important for when i need to print a graph from my TI-89 Titanium

imo, i think that a standard install of vista looks much better than a standard install of ubuntu

microsoft is a master in marketing its products (and making huge profits)

### Post by Patrick Snyder on 2009-08-03
> **waqar said:**
> If people hate Microsoft, what about Google?
They are both monopolies, but it seems to be Microsoft's policy to crush and destroy in order to get/stay ahead whereas Google's history is much more of a "do-no-evil" bulldozer.

[LIST]
[*]Surprisingly little innovation has actually come out of Microsoft.  They've mostly stolen concepts and destroyed competition  (ex: DOS, Netscape, and many aspects of Windows itself)

[*]A surprisingly amount of innovation has actually come out of Google, and when competition gets in the way, they buy it, making the creators rich (ex: YouTube, Blogger, etc)


[*]Microsoft attempts to lock you into their products.
[*]Google does the opposite.  There's nothing keeping people using Google and most of their products except for superior quality.


[*]Microsoft is constantly in legal battles throughout the world due to illegal anti-competitive practices.

[*]Google has been around for a shorter period, but has only recently gone under comparatively minor investigation over some hiring practices.  There just doesn't seem to be much they're doing illegally or immorally.


[*]Watch Microsoft CEO Steve Ballmer on youtube and he often focuses on pushing ahead and succeeding as a business.  This might be what the stock holders want to hear, but it does not translate well for the users as they'll avoid problems and stagnate innovation if it increases profits.

[*]Watch Google's CEOs Sergey Brin and Larry Page and they often talk about following their interests.  In fact there's a company policy called "20% time" where an employee has to work 1 day out of the 5-day week on his own project.  Many of Google's innovations come out of their employees following their interests.  This translates well for the end user as the focus turns to making quality, usable things that the developers themselves have a personal vested interest in.
[/LIST]

They are both monopolies, but how they use that power is very, very different.

---

From what I can tell, Google can't lock you in to their products. People use them because they want to. I still use Windows, because I have to.

### Post by Katalog on 2009-09-18
> **Dragonbite said:**
> Having an opinion for something, like Linux, is not difficult and it is easy to then demonize and put down the "competition", like Microsoft, at every turn.

Unfortunately this is also part of the criteria of a fanboy, or fanatic.

The question is, can you say something positive aboue Microsoft? Can you actually "see" from the other person's point of view?

Since there are so many arguments both for- and against-Microsoft there has to be some merit to both sides of the coin, but can you find it?

I've noticed a growing anti-Microsoft feeling but I wonder if it is blind following the herd like sheep or are you actually making a choice?

> **hungrywolf9 said:**
> I'm glad someone has brought this argument up as I think it is a very valid. I must first say that I do love Linux and have been a Linux user and promoter for years now. However, I must say that I also have to use Windows for my work as some of the tools I use only exist in windows. Although I hate the windows environment because of all the maintenance issues and pain of looking after such PCs I must say that the new windows 7 may have most of these problems licked. I like it's scalability and the way it manages the system. Having said that, to me it seems to be following a more Linux path than windows. Perhaps the tides have turned without we knowing it.
I only wish there is more operating systems out there for me to play with and learn from. Who knows perhaps one day soon another break through will happen.

Reasons for liking or disliking a company can be based on many factors, and a lot of them have been stated prior to this post. It could be because some people are indeed sheep and all "the cool kids" are Anti-Microsoft, or you are simply so Pro-Linux, that you just naturally hate MS. Point being, that everyone has their *own* opinion,  or none at all. What follows here is mine.

Up until now, most of my replies to this thread have been tongue-in-cheek, and have had a smart alec, flippant tone to them. But if you want to get down to brass tacks and hear the actual truth of the matter, I will tell you right here and now in no uncertain terms that in all honestly I hate Microsoft with a passion that is unreal, based not on being a fanboy, but almost purely on principle, and that being that they are blatantly *anti-choice* and *anti-freedom*. The plain truth of the matter is that they would be the only operating system on the planet if they had the legal means to eliminate and crush the competition, and that is a fact. Even Ballmer himself has said so, in not so many careful words (but anyone who refers to Linux as "a cancer" is a &^%$! in my book to begin with). Every time you turn around, they get caught with their pants down around their ankles, up to their same old anti-competiitive practices, or doing something new (or old) to outright try and discredit  or cripple Linux and free software. The Best Buy training thing is pretty small potatoes, IMO. The stuff that really matters and is an especially good example are the patents which OIN recently acquired, much to MS chagrin, since MS intended to sell them to patent trolls who would then use them to bring lawsuits against Linux vendors and distributors in court. That way, they once again get to damage Linux, while at the same time keeping their hands clean because they are not the ones directly responsible for bringing legal action against the parties involved. Here they are selling these patents to the highest bidder (which, by the way, they would not allow OIN to attend this auction and refused them an invitation), while at the same time smiling broadly and trumpeting a new age of cooperation with "CodePlex". It's a win/win situation for both parties. The patent companies, whose sole purpose is to profit from suing other corporations, get their payday and MS gets to hurt the competition while maintaining a neutral public face. If you like Microsoft and want to use their products, that is your choice and there is nothing that I can or would do to stop you, but in my mind if you are giving them a single cent you are spending it to support one of the most evil, underhanded corporations in existence. To paraphrase a passage I read on GrokLaw recently, this just about sums it up:

*** "Microsoft need to be treated in harmony with their actions, not their words. They hate FOSS, and they show it. They will kill it, if you relax and let them. They secretly attack Linux while publicly claiming to want interoperability. They are what they are, and_ hating their methods is not a disease. It's the reality principle_."***

I don't think I could describe it any better than that statement does right there. That pretty much wraps up my opinion of  MS into a tidy little nutshell. For me, there is nothing positive I can point to regarding MS, but there certainly are two sides to the coin (ala Harvey Dent), as MS is just about as two faced as they come. As the quote above says, they are what the are, and if you believe otherwise, IMO, you are either kidding yourself or turning a blind eye and not caring, as is unfortunately the case with many other causes worth standing up for in this world. All of the "positive" things they have done to "cooperate" with free software or contribute to Linux are nothing more than a token farce to help keep them from looking like the bad guy, nothing more, nothing less. And it is my view that those who buy into their hype will live to see it blow up in their face like a trick cigar at some point in the not so distant future. But that's simply *my* raw, unadulterated personal opinion, FWIW, and you know what they say about opinions.

### Post by Penguin Guy on 2009-10-10
I wouldn't say I 'hated' it, but here are a few things:
[LIST]
[*]Name - Very original and creative, it's going to be *so* easy to trademark it.
[*]Fragmentation - no computer geek feels satisfied without running a defrag every other day.
[*]Filesystem support - the wide range of supported filesystems makes it a very compatible operating system.
[*]Closed-Source - I don't want anyone messing around with the source code of *my* computer!
[*]Non-POSIX - Why bother following the POSIX standard? Show them who's boss!
[*]Windows is a 64 bit tweak of a 32 bit extension to a 16 bit user interface for an 8 bit operating system based on a 4 bit architecture - an impressive history.
[/LIST]

### Post by AgentZ86 on 2009-12-31
> **Dragonbite said:**
> Having an opinion for something, like Linux, is not difficult and it is easy to then demonize and put down the "competition", like Microsoft, at every turn.

Unfortunately this is also part of the criteria of a fanboy, or fanatic.

The question is, can you say something positive aboue Microsoft? Can you actually "see" from the other person's point of view?

Since there are so many arguments both for- and against-Microsoft there has to be some merit to both sides of the coin, but can you find it?

I've noticed a growing anti-Microsoft feeling but I wonder if it is blind following the herd like sheep or are you actually making a choice?

I don't hate MS and am proud of Bill Gates for a fine job growing his business.

I have no problem with that, I do have some problems with the operations and some policies etc, but I could care less about criticizing the company and the hard working people at MS. Perhaps some policy makers could use a good talking, but hey it's their business.

I can say I just don't like the OS regarding the viruses,freezups,lockups and the problems with conflicting dll's that get overwritten without my permissions which may be from a new game installed or perhaps a printer driver that wants to update a dll to their version of the dll etc. which causes all kinds of problems.

Anyhow, If windows did not let me down so many times with my data and even 1 game that I liked to play I probably would have continued to use it, but I just could not deals with some of those things.

Having said that I can say that I really like the windows feel and look and the way it is suppose to operate.
When a computer is running windows and it's working properly it's very hard to beat. I really use to enjoy it at times.

However, anytime something had to go into the computer update or even just a simple plugin, the computer never really could handle things properly. Starts with errors to a gamut of issues there is no real reliability and ends with a reload of the OS on a regular basis, after searching for dll errors and multiple forums to find known solutions for known problems.

These sort of things can still happen linux, however when you run linux apps that are maintained then you have the stability that cannot be matched.

The only issue seems to be when people use non-maintained sources or try to customize things beyond what has been proven when it creates problems.

If Windows would do that same sort of proving that the maintainers of linux do I believe it could be much less problems.

But my praise to Bill Gates and MS for the invention which really got things rolling.
I wish he would get more involved with linux open source and complete his mission to have a computer on every desk.
He's already got the money now back to the dream.

Happy posting

Just because Linux has had so much technology ** Microsoft got their hands on itdoesn't mean Microsoft didn't still put in the work to implement some extra features to climb a little higher in usability compared to Linux in the past 10 years.**

Yeah, that is called "[Embrace, Extend, Extinguish]("http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Embrace,_extend_and_extinguish")" and is very vicious strategy. It basically goes something like this:

**1.** They pick one open standard that they do not support
**2.** Then, instead  of supporting that standard, they make proprietary version of it from scratch, burning huge amounts of money on marketing in the process. 
**3.** Then they make their proprietary standard remotely compatible with open one, just to compete better. 
**4.** Marketing Marketing, Marketing. Making Microsoft zealots llook good.
**5.** They put in some more features, to make open standard look bad compared with their crap. Then they threat to sue anybody who tries to compete by implementing  equal features in open standard.
**6.** More marketing, blackmailing people to convert, buying OEMs support and spreading FUD about open standard. 
**7.** Then they buy some company who had been huge supporter of open standard, just to make that open standard look humiliated. 
**8.** When they seize huge market share, they drop any compatibility with open standard and say that open standard do not want to play nice with their proprietary crap. And people believe, and one who say that M$ is the one who do not want to play nice get called "haters", "fanbois", "biased", etc.
**9.** World domination
**10.** Find another standard to overtake. 


Microsoft pretty much destroys everything they get their hands on.


@Dragonbite

Sorry for saying your thread is pointless, I did not read first post correctly. I previously thought it was Microsoft praising thread.

### Post by dln9 on 2010-01-03
I guess I never thought about it before, but I now realize that I am a "Microsoft hater".  

I used Microsoft extensively, both at work and at home, for about 20 years.  Last year I made the complete switch to Ubuntu.  

Ubuntu does *everything* I need, everything I want, everything I did with Microsoft.  

Getting to learn Ubuntu has been hard.  Figuring out problems has been very difficult at times, requiring a lot of time, a lot of research, a lot of swearing.  I do *not* like that, I wish it weren't so hard, problems in Ubuntu are not fun.  

But - I had the *exact* same experiences with Microsoft.  Stuff would break, go wrong, etc., and I would go through the same hell.  

The difference:  Microsoft wants to charge me money to use their product and go through this hell.  With Ubuntu, I can go through the same hell, and eventually get the same working result, for free.  

The choice was easy for me.  

When I finally realized that there was a viable alternative to Microsoft (Ubuntu) that is free, is when I think that I began to hate Microsoft.  

I wish nearly everyone would switch from Microsoft to something else.  I don't want them all to switch to just Ubuntu - I want to see a flourishing variety of operating systems - yes, even including a remnant that stays with Microsoft.  

I derive immense personal satisfaction when I succeed in luring someone away from Microsoft.

### Post by yester64 on 2010-01-03
> **Dragonbite said:**
> Having an opinion for something, like Linux, is not difficult and it is easy to then demonize and put down the "competition", like Microsoft, at every turn.

Unfortunately this is also part of the criteria of a fanboy, or fanatic.

The question is, can you say something positive aboue Microsoft? Can you actually "see" from the other person's point of view?

Since there are so many arguments both for- and against-Microsoft there has to be some merit to both sides of the coin, but can you find it?

I've noticed a growing anti-Microsoft feeling but I wonder if it is blind following the herd like sheep or are you actually making a choice?

Why people hate usually Mircosoft?
That goes back to the early days and has something to do with a cooperation which essentially took over computer business.
How many companies were placed out of business because of microsoft? There are a lot. And it did not have anything to do with that they made bad business decisions.
I remember that in the late 80s you had a lot of hate against M$ because other OS's did not prevail in the turmoil that erupted due bad business decisions (Commodore, Atari).
Of course there is a lot of bias. And surprisingly now one would suspect Google of such bad traits.
A lot of hate is but also a result of how M$ turned out to be what they became.
Look at all the OS's they released, and all the applications they sell. Even if one will admit that there are some good features in it, they are bloated and not save like in security.
Remember the time ActiveX was a hate acronym? Not so long ago indeed.
M$ always pushed to have their format to be called the standard to everyone on the planet.
The funny thing is, that most people who hail M$ are the ones who pirated the software and did not even buy it.
M$ has not done a lot to proof that their software gotten any better. Instead it copies or imitates Mac OS for a long time. Or if you argue, made their own version of Xerox OS.
The one thing that is always funny, are the disclaimer you have to sign off. Well, that is in general in the closed system world and not just M$.
It is not that i hate M$. I simply do not want to use it after i had almost a decade to live with it. And not to forget that you pay a hefty price for the upgrade.
If you have to use it, i pity you. But if you want to use it because you think its better, life with it.
There are not many choice out there beside Windows. Not everyone knows Linux and certainly not a lot of people want to deal with Linux anyway.
For the rest there is OSX which is superior to Windows in any way.
Why does Windows still have the Registry? A concept that can make your system so unstable and in the worst case, not even run anymore.
Problem for M$ is, that the OS is not as relevant as it once was. A lot of people got stuck to XP (kinda outdated) and don't switch to 7. And i don't think that this will change.
The biggest problem is in my view, that M$ did not encourage innovation. How long do we have 64bit cpu's but are still strapped in 32bit code.
My conclusion was 2 years ago, that it was a waste of money to buy new hardware and be stuck with a infirour OS. Very few application use 64bit at all, and almost no games anyway.
And i don't buy the argument with marketshare. If thats the case, then there will be no innovation in the future.
Thats my take.
So i am not really a hater, but i certainly stay away from M$ and its superior applications.

Windows itself has its attributes as does any other OS. The main thing is it is very expensive to own and operate because to actually acomplish anything you need to buy so much software, not just the OS. Also to protect you machine is expensive.
So even thought Windows has it's attribute the expenses is not worth it when you can do 90% + with Linux with less worry, security, and expenses.

### Post by The_Pirate_King on 2010-01-12
Well I certainly can say something positive about microsoft.  Or semi-positive. 

Microsoft has been in the business for years.  If you buy a computer with microsoft installed, you'll usually get all the drivers you need with it, prepackaged and ready to go.  Since very few PCs come prepackaged with linux, it tends to be more difficult to set up a working linux install.  
Another thing is compatibility.  Microsoft is, admittedly, compatible with a very wide range of software and games that it is extremely difficult to run on Linux.  

Many linux users argue that this is "not Linux's fault", that it's the people who program the games for windows and the people who assemble and sell PCs that should be blamed for making things difficult for Linux users.  
And while this may be somewhat true, the fact of the matter remains that the big bully corporation manages to make things easier for its users through sheer economic force.

---

### Post by airtonix on 2010-01-19
Positive : 
+ They make good documentation ( but only in areas where they want you to become educated ).
 
Negative : 
+ Pity they didn't incorporate taekwindow and various implementations of the "window utilities" from compiz into windows 7.
+ Also, they still don't make theming the window manager as easy as it is in linux. I ended up installing litestep on windows 7 and still couldn't theme the window manager in an easy fashion.
+ Doesn't have a satisfactory virtual files system, meaning you can not mount a remote drive and have it appear as a local filesyste, like FUSE on linux does. It also means there are virtually no third party transparet extensions for windows explorer that attach other remote file systems to the tree (like bluetooth storage systems).

A simple way to explain this is Windows fully loaded used 30% of a machines resources and Linux fully loaded is 50% with less code.

### Post by 12_String on 2010-01-31
I guess I am not really a part of the MicroSoft hater group, but I use xubuntu because it is free. I do miss the familiarity of Windows, because being so new to xubuntu I feel lost all the time. I am not a programmer and do not write commands; heck I wouldn't even know where to go to learn how. But for just doing things like posting documents onto websites, or even pasting text into an email, I find I have a TON of trouble with xubuntu. My Yahoo! email does not allow pasting from xubuntu but it does from a computer with MicroSoft in it. My wordpress.com blog does not allow me to paste or to view videos or pdf files with xubuntu, but it does when I am at a computer that has MicroSoft in it. So I am beginning to dislike xubuntu. If there were not the difficulties associated with it I would love it.

### Post by sudoer541 on 2010-02-02
every OS has it own flaws

windows:
expensive
too many versions 
pc requirements increase once new version of windows comes out. 
in some cases insecure (if you are not careful)
unstable (rarely)
slow and needs maintenance


Mac os x (I havent used it but here is my general idea and may not be accurate)
locked in its own hardware (no pc version)
if your hardware breaks bye bye OSX
not a lot of software and games but they are getting there fast (congrats apple!)


linux:
not very user friendly (you have to mess with the terminal at times)
too many versions/flavours 
not many software makers support it
not many hardware makers officially support it
lacks gaming
lacks popularity
locked version of software eg firefox (no easy install process available *easy as in point and click or a downloadable package *)

again this is my point of view and it may not necessarily agree with your point of view. 
if you are trolling, you know where the close button is.

### Post by Rocky_Reid on 2010-02-18
I think the fact Bill changed the entire planet is an awesome accomplishment. However it would have happened anyway. I choose to use Ubuntu because I find it very dependable, even if I am trying to learn, and destroy my OS, I can have all programs/repositories up and running in about an hour. (Try doing that with windows after rebooting countless times for updates) I think they have made some very smart decisions in being aggressive in the business world which inherently transcends into the household. People like what they know. I was apprehensive about changing to Linux but I finally got so tired of crashes, bugs, registry problems etc... I tried it. I can honestly say I do miss the games but that is not a OS benefit that is Bill's big dollars controlling the market. However I have turned my focus from games to actually learning things such as programming, a more productive use of my time, so that is a positive thing that comes with my switch to Linux. I for one do not ever see myself leaving the Linux system I really enjoy it. I am looking at the Nokia N900 now as a phone option because it runs on a Linux kernel. (and that phone is SICK... LOL) All in all these discussions mean very little. If you like it use it, they all have good and bad. Do what makes you happy just don't complain when you are rebooting 394579357938 times while doing updates :)

### Post by Dragonbite on 2010-02-18
Has anybody tried watching the Olympics online?

I know there's a beta of Moonlight to watch it in Linux (which does work), but my desktop has a bigger screen than my laptop so I watched it in Windows 7.

Watching those videos was 100% better than watching anything in Adobe Flash. 

I don't have a super-fast DSL connection, so usually once I am able to I have to pause the Flash player and give it time to buffer the video before trying to play it, otherwise it comes all choppy when the playback reaches the end of the buffer.

I did not have that with the Silverlight player for the Olympics.  Once I got any sort of indicator of non-smooth playback, I started with pausing the video clip for 1 second before hitting play and that fixed it.

I know there are a lot of other factors and technologies involved (the pipe from NBC, fewer users, video playter programming, etc.) but that is by far the best video experience I have seen online.

### Post by Flymo on 2010-04-13
My first MS purchase was EditASM for the TRS-80 - a clever piece of work, but not what I needed, since it ate 'so much'  RAM :) and insisted on abstracting physcal addresses to label-relative references. Not so useful when you are driving I/O ports on a Z80.   
DOS was OK, but not up to CP/M quality.  Miles behind the slick ROM-based aftermarket office and development kit on the 'hobbyist'  Amstrad CPCs and BBC Micros. Protext etc.
Early Windows lagged severely behind (eg) GEM, which had a proper file manager, and later Windows versions caught up slowly.  
Cannot remember being enthused about the tech merit of any Win release......    oh, hang on, Win 3 would run Corel Draw, which was a goodie, if a little unstable.   And there was OLE in there that let you do all sorts of sneaky things in documents that are trivial today.
Publisher was pretty good in the late '90s, but none of the Wndows OS stuff was ever stable until a year+ after release, at least not on our machines. And MS have been losing the Virus/Malware battle bigtime - after missing the boat on the Internet they also missed the obvious implications. Bad call

Looking back, it was obvious even in the 1980s that Unix was a superior environment, and I even spent my own money on Coherent some 25+ years ago - wrote a fair bit of code on my 386,  wrassled with uucp config and re-wrote the device drivers for  the 16450 UART.  GNU and Linux together have brought open source malleability to a superior environment.  

Even Apple spotted it, and pillaged the Unix-based NeXT project to build OS X. Very good call, that.  OS X is closed, and some of its oddities may never be fixed because of that, but it is still a neat (but costly) Unix derivative.

The MS reps were trying to foist Win NT on us at work decades back - NT 3.1 et seq - on the promise that it was a 'clean-room' clone of Unix.  Their mendacity was their own undoing.  Since then we have seen NT 4,  NT 5 (aka Win 2K) NT 5.1 (aka Win XP), NT 6 (Vista) and NT 7, err, Windows 7*.  Of them all,  5.0 and 5.1 stand out in hindsight;  but they both took a few years to stabilise.

*I was wrong!  Win 7 is in fact NT 6.1, it turns out....  But 6+1=7 - errr....  in Microsoft Maths? ;)

MS did make some nice mice, but we abandoned them for trackballs after going into CAD, and their curvy keyboard is pretty good too. The joysticks used to be good, then went downhill fast and were, I think, withdrawn.

*My favourite Windows?*  **TIny XP** running safely inside **KVM** under **Ubuntu**!  With web  access disabled, a minimal configuration and no need for aftermarket anti-malware scanning every drive/web access, XP is at least as fast inside Ubuntu/KVM as on bare metal, for many (non-game) applications. But, having fathomed out how to do it a year or so back, we rarely use it now.

Power to Billy and Melinda for spending their zillions wisely,  but their business methods left a lot to be desired.

ymmv, Ben

---

### Post by techn0mad on 2010-04-17
> **smartboyathome said:**
> Without Microsoft, we wouldn't be where we are today, and the GUI would probably be very different. Our GUI was influenced a lot by both Microsoft and Apple.

Not a Microsoft hater, just felt like posting that. ;)

Umm, you should do some homework.  :) The GUI paradigm that Microsoft, Apple, Linux, UNIX etc. are all using was the development by [Xerox PARC]("http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PARC_%28company%29") of an idea published by [SRI]("http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SRI_International") in the late 1960s. Read up on a couple of guys named [Douglas Engelbart]("http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Douglas_Engelbart") and Alan Kay.

Apple more or less copied the Xerox GUI for it's Lisa and then Macintosh systems, and the Windows GUI started as a crude bolt-on for DOS, also copied from Xerox/Apple.

---

### Post by Ranko Kohime on 2010-05-08
> **Dragonbite said:**
> I've noticed a growing anti-Microsoft feeling but I wonder if it is blind following the herd like sheep or are you actually making a choice?
For me, I started on Windows.  I spent a year or so on 3.11, another two years on 98, then 2000, then XP, before finally giving up in 2006, shutting down my last PC and going strictly Mac.

There was absolutely no professionalism in the design of those versions.  It was like the project of a programming major getting failing grades.  Numerous compatibility issues, the simple fact that it did not know what a generic driver was or how to install it without manual control, (and why DID you have to install a driver each time you plugged the same peripheral into a different port?!), the crashes, of course.  Just not what I expect from a company that somehow managed a 95% share of the OS market.

More recently I've learned that Microsoft is in bed with the government to make spying on Windows computers easier.  That pretty much sealed the deal for me.

Now I have heard reports that 7 is a huge leap for them, and perhaps they have actually gotten a passing grade this time.  But the spy crap is keeping me away.

So for me, definitely a choice.

At the time I killed off my last PC, Linux was still in what I consider it's childhood, and was quite difficult for me to use.  Since then it's become easier, and I've become a more advanced user.  Though, still a user am I.

Now Linux is to the point of maturity that I'm seriously considering building myself a new desktop PC, to replace my aging Mac.

### Post by McRat on 2010-05-09
> **jrothwell97 said:**
> Not entirely fair.

The computer can only work as fast as the human that's operating it: I'd be willing to bet that a good amount of those 20 minutes were spent typing in the test results.

Absolutely.

If you have an old DOS machine with a 386DX, load Lotus 1-2-3 and see how long basic computer functions take.  As the old adage goes, Intel Giveth, Microsoft Taketh Away.

Microsoft is a well managed company.  But innovation was never their strong suit.  They mostly monetize other peoples successful technologies.

Their biggest contribution to the desktop was perhaps simplifying networking.  Before W3.11, there was Netware, and several peer-to-peer systems out for the i86 machines, but MS integrated it into the O/S and made a networked computer the norm, even for household use.

And MS Professional Basic 7.0 is still just about the fastest coding tool ever.  If you haven't used it, it's QBasic on steroids. The output is compiled code that is as fast as compiled C code without the need to compile between executions when developing.

But these were improvements on other people's work.

Some people think that Microsoft is responsible for the Desktop Revolution.  No.  There were lots of companies in the running, and only Apple is left today.  IBM, Zerox, Digital Research, Radio Shack, Commodore, Atari, etc.

Microsoft is responsible for the fact there is 1 company with over 90% control over the industry.  They were very good at snuffing out competitors.  OS/2 did not die a natural death, it was just another tombstone in Redmond cemetery.

While there are hundreds of examples, let's look at the basic office functions:  Word Processing, Spreadsheets, Databases.

Word Processing was dominated by Word Perfect, but had a lot of competition.  MS used their control over the O/S, and pre-packaging to push Word to the top.  Many professional DP operators believe WP was a better, faster tool.

Spreadsheets were dominated by Lotus 123.  Written in assembler, it was blazing fast.  Again, MS control over the O/S feature set, and "special undocumented functions" pushed their slower Excel product to the top.

Databases are bizarre.  It is possible that MS actually killed off this application family for desktops.  Most machines had DBase on them, it was a normal application to have on a desktop.   MS released Access which was clumsy, slow, and buggy.  Out came FoxPro and quickly became the best product in the market.  So MS bought FoxPro and erased the company.  By now DBase was all but dead, so there was nothing left.  Most computers no longer have a dbase product on them.

It is entirely possible that MS stagnated the desktop industry instead of making it grow.  It was going to grow no matter what, so perhaps MS's focus on eliminating competition instead of pushing innovation actually hurt all of us.  

Perhaps their enduring legacy will be how they forced the hardware makers to crank up the speed to accomodate their bloatware.

---

Yeah. Good things about Windows include that its convenient to go with the flow. Its easy to install from CD without an Internet connection. Learning curve is shallow. Good game selection. Good commercial software selection.


The benefits of Windows are well known. We hear them every day. That doesnt mean we have to like them or think its worth what we give up.

Its not worth the price tag.
Its not worth the lack of free software.
Its not worth the lack of freedom
Its not worth the lack of security
Its not worth the lack of privacy
Its not worth the diminishing performance
Its not worth the viruses
Its not worth having to degrag


But yeah, without an internet connection Windows has some advantages for people who have to work offline and install from CD. 

If there were some better standards, or if more dependencies were provided (Call it bloat, but I think MOST people can afford the hard disk space as long as it doesnt eat up ram or CPU power....MOST people would give up an extra 4gb of hdd if it meant more plug and play and being able to install deb files from CD).....yeah, there are convenient things about Windows, but there are all things that **** me the **** off. 


When you lose a CD in windows its actually harder to track down the drivers than it is in Ubuntu. Ubuntu in many ways is more plug and play than windows is.

IMHO

[I]On another note: There is a new concern out there that I'm seeing that  may take the wind out of the Windows7 sails. Is that with the economy in  the failing state that it is most of the customers I'm coming in  contact with are incredibly tight with their cash. So upgrade or  replacement of workstations with the new W7 may not happen like  Microsoft wishes.
[/I] 
Perceptive point. Business is very wary now of spending megabucks on new hardware / software every 3 - 5 years. The sought functionality for any business is quite stable and long term. Corporate systems nowadays tend to be ordered by desired desktop functionality - usually in generic terms which are independent of the platform to be installed. Corporate customers now buy OA systems which can handled their legacy information and can inter-operate with their partners. They are as unlikely to specify 'A Windows Network delivering Microsoft Office 200n' as they are to ask for 'A Ubuntu network delivering Open Office'. I suspect that Microsoft are discovering that they are losing out to cheaper GNU/Linux based systems in the corporate market space, because suppliers are offering the cheapest implementations to address their customers functional specifications.

rjbl

### Post by murderslastcrow on 2010-08-29
Aero, while it is heavy, tends to run smooth on all hardware so far. This is, of course, due to vendor support which is about equal on Linux, but not perfected so far in compositing rendering. I'm mainly referring to KDE 4 on NVIDIA cards compared to Windows 7. They're pretty much the same, but there is a small noticeable different in framerates. I don't notice it on ATi cards.

Aside from that, Windows can run a lot of programs Wine still can't. Everyone who's been on Windows for years has some niche program whose alternative they are wary of, even if it's better.

So I guess what I mean is, even if Wine increases the amount of software available to Linux users, Windows today still has more of a chance (almost certain) to run a given Windows program. I'm not sure if this is really that big of a deal unless it's a business application or specific game whose story cannot be replaced, as Linux has tools for every task, for free. It's almost a no brainer, but people are allowed to be picky.

To be honest, every Windows user that I try to play the 'not just open source, but free of cost' card on says to me, 'well, for me Windows and <insert application here> are free anyway *wink*'.

I'm the only person I know who isn't a pirate of some sort. I find it very amusing that everyone else seems to be just fine stealing everything, and then Windows lovers who have actually stolen the OS and all the programs they run put me down for being a 'freetard'.

But honestly, other than a few rendering issues here and there, and application availability, Windows still runs. Just because in every other area Linux outperforms Windows doesn't mean that Windows doesn't have merit in that it performs at all.

### Post by libssd on 2010-08-29
> **Dragonbite said:**
> The question is, can you say something positive aboue Microsoft? Can you actually "see" from the other person's point of view?
Sure, because of its dominant position, you have a much greater chance of a MS OS running with full hardware support; Linux is almost always playing catch-up in this area (witness all the driver problems for people with Nvidia graphics cards). Buy a new computer, plug it in, and Windows 7 works. Install any Linux distro, and maybe it does or maybe it doesn't.

### Post by beew on 2010-08-30
> So I guess what I mean is, even if Wine increases the amount of software  available to Linux users, Windows today still has more of a chance  (almost certain) to run a given Windows programNot only "windows programs", all the best open source programs that run on Linux are available to windows as well: openoffice, the Gimp, vlc player, audacity .. you name it.  While porting the best open source programs to Windows may show Windows users the value of open source, but ironically it also puts Linux in greater disadvantage in terms of software availability : it  creates a situation where windows gets the best of both worlds in terms of softwares, by running windows you get the best proprietary AND open source softwares.  I really hope FOSS developers working on Linux applications would spend less time trying to port their applications to windows and instead use the time to improve their features.

### Post by libssd on 2010-08-31
> **Tracy177 said:**
> u never should say people hate Microsoft or Windows. Windows is everything we got today. mac linux still cant compare. 

windows = plug and play
Plug and pray is more like it. My own experience, as well as every comparison review I have seen place still Mac OS X ahead of Windows in functionality, consistency, ease of use, and stability. Each new version of Windows generally seems about one release behind OS X.

> windows=when system crash u can use restore point or just dvd and repair system i never used it cuz none windows on my pc never crashed

ubuntu=no restore point no recovery mode very big possibility of system crashing and if u dont know the right commands u will have to reinstall everything again + download all updates.


That's what backups are for, and anybody who runs any OS without making restorable backups gets what he deserves when disaster strikes. Windows has restore points because it is so unreliable; you never know if it's going to work after applying software updates. Previous kernels are listed in Grub until you manually delete them. I have had Windows machines go south (including inability to boot from the last known working configuration) after applying MS service pack updates. I have never lost a Mac or Linux machine except through my own stupidity. 

> windows= microsoft office 
ubuntu=open office but cant compare to microsoft office
You're right -- the Office 2007 and above user interface is so bad it's reason in itself to use almost any other platform. I hated at first sight the new, screen hogging, feature hiding "ribbon" features introduced with Office 2007. Ironically, MS Office for OS X has a far better user interface than the Windows version. 

There are definitely features in MS Office that aren't available in other office suites, but as someone who supported end users for more than 25 years, it is my experience that *almost nobody uses these advanced features*. It almost made me cry to see people using Tab Tab Tab Tab to right-align text, the space bar to center text, or Return Return Return to force a page break, or typing header and footer information manually on each page -- yet I have seen each of these behaviors by highly educated people who have used MS Word for decades. The most important thing the advanced features do is bloat the software.

---

### Post by Dragonbite on 2010-08-31

> **Tracy177 said:**
> windows=when system crash u can use restore point or just dvd and repair system i never used it cuz none windows on my pc never crashed
ubuntu=no restore point no recovery mode very big possibility of system crashing and if u dont know the right commands u will have to reinstall everything again + download all updates.
With Windows XP I got a BSOD during bootup and could not get it to run.  Repair didn't work either.  I used Ubuntu to run a live CD, forced mount the XP partition and copied all of the files from the (dead) XP system to the server.

Do a full backup on the /home directory using something like Deja Dup, including config files modified and use Synaptic to auto-update a text file with the applications you install.  PPAs may cause a problem but nothing is 100%.[LIST=1]
[*]Reinstall the latest Ubuntu, without having to deal with any activation codes, etc.
[*]Install Deja Dup or other backup software used (Grsync? CrashPlan? etc.)
[*]Run the synaptic and import the text file of applications to get all of the applications previously installed
[*]At the same time, use backup program's restore option to restore /home files (and thus configurations as well)
[/LIST]
I am even looking at using CrashPlan to backup from Linux systems online.
> **Tracy177 said:**
> ubuntu=unless u have old sound card it wont work out of the box u'll have to look for correct drivers install it download 200mb updates and than it maybe will work i tried intel acl1200 and it didnt work out of the box not on 10.04 nor on 9.10 nor on 10.10 
ati hdmi is not supported

But on a thread on OOO someone who seemed to know MS Office and OpenOffice capabilities very well,--at least that is my impression as well as others',--said Basic (VBA and OO basic) is crap anyway and you run the risk of picking up real bad habits if that is your first exposure to programming.  He said python is a lot faster, more powerful and flexible and OO.O supports that. What do you think?
Experienced or not, sounds like opinion to me.

"VBA runs the risk of picking up real bad habits..." is true of any programs you start getting into. Because it is so easily accessible (install Office, got VBA) there is a good chance that somebody develops bad habits, but making it more stringent just increases the chance they never get into programming in the first place!  

Excel's VBA got me started before I moved to Access & VBA, then ASP and SQL Server and now to ASP.NET.  So for me, VBA has been good (though I am in the process of trying to learn C# and to cross-pollinate with Mono).

### Post by Nick_Jinn on 2010-09-01
I just barely decided to really learn to use my computer....for the time being....I need my crutches. I prefer using a GUI when I can and I like things to be intuitive and easy to use.


There are other things about Microsoft I just dont like though....its not that they are lacking ease of use, but there is a lot of maintenance and cost involved, and I dont like how they corner the market sometimes using means I consider unethical....though others will just call it 'business'....I would be willing to take a hit in performance and ease of use in exchange for freedom and privacy and security....but thanks to advances in Linux, much of which is owed to Debian and Ubuntu and devs downstream from them, I actually feel like I am in many ways getting better performance...at least I have choice. 

If I use Windows I cant just decide to download a light weight version if the default is a little heavy for my system.....one of my classes "upgraded" from XP to 7 with only 1gb of ram....not happy with the results....If that was Linux I could just swich to LXDE...With Windows they would have to redo the whole network.


But yes, there are in fact convenient things about Windows, but in my opinion its not worth the trade off.


I do still have a copy for old games, but more and more I just buy whats compatible with Linux and Wine and ignore the rest.

It works the other way round here: Windows 7 needed drivers for the sound card to be recognized correctly (only playback but no recording with the "default" W7 driver) and for the video card. ### Post by Thoric on 2010-09-01
A positive.. Well my preferred audio sequencing & mastering software doesn't run well at all in any flavor of Linux I've tried. Be it wine or crossover. Anything other than VM falters to the point of unusability.

Hardware compatability is a little bit stronger. I've still yet to get my bluetooth mouse to function outside of XP/7. My cheap $30 MIDI keyboard also won't work at all in Ubuntu. Granted a fly by night sort of company manufactured it & it was never intended to function outside of a useless "learn to play" software suite.

I don't care about off console gaming so I've not experienced that side of the argument. So I'm just going to take word for it.. However this also falls into software compatibility.

So I managed to come up with 2 positives for the OS.

On the flip side, I couldn't get my new printer to network using XP. Took about an hour to get things working right with 7. But eventually did mostly work. Functioned flawlessly within 2 minutes using samba under Ubuntu.

I no longer have to make a near to daily backup of the hard disk my OS  runs on. Although System Restore did seem to work fairly well in place  of a full backup. I've yet to have a crash of any sort in Linux while using stable software releases. If there is a crash things are usually minimal and only affect the application & not the functionality of the entire OS. 

In order to get any performance quality out of the machine. I don't need to waste time removing junk that you're forced during installation process.

I actually have control over everything within the OS. All be it that is also a con if you're not careful. Best analogy I can use for Winblows.. Well, would you buy a car with the hood welded shut? Sure you can tinker around underneath. But good luck changing your spark plugs..

Off the top of my head nothing else comes to mind that doesn't one way or another fall into those "categories". So 2-4.. 50/50.. That is to say without adding in the frequency of issue variable.

The company on the whole however. Well I almost feel obligated to respect them. Through smart business & fairly faulty software. Microsoft has not only managed there way to the top but remained there for a number of years now. One thing that may surprise you about this powerhouse. They actually have a department dedicated to redistribute the money they gorged out of their consumers back into the community. I've not heard of any other company actually having a depot for charitable outflow. Granted a good portion of their charitable donations consist of computers loaded with Winblows. But nevertheless that still brings computers to many schools & organizations that would likely otherwise go without.

Alright so now I'm babbling.. Point I want to get accross is that I don't hate Microsoft. I just really dislike the dependability of their OS's. Which almost isn't always unreliable.

### Post by beew on 2010-09-01
> One One thing that may surprise you about this powerhouse. They actually having a depot for charitable outflow. Granted a good portion of their charitable donations consist of computers loaded with Winblows. 

Not surprising to me at all. This is just brand promotion and a way to lock in users. It is very cheap as a marketing scheme to throw out a few crumbs to ensure there is an ongoing supply of users who know only how to use windows for their work and to sustain a computing environment (hardware and software) in the workplace that cannot function without windows. I bet all these "donations" are tax deductible too so it costs MS nothing and gets them some PR points to boot. 

> But nevertheless that still brings computers to many schools & organizations that would likely otherwise go without.

I don't buy that. Without MS dangling a carrot ahead of them these schools and organizations would likely consider other options which would be better for them (and bad for Ms) in the long run.

They could probably easily switch to Linux if not because of the fear of the unknown and human inertia. This would cost them no licensing fee at all and since Linux works so well with older hardwares it would also save them a lot of upgrading costs which would be required to run the latest windows. 

In many jurisdictions outside the U.S. they are already making the switch because they can't afford the cost of relying on MS.

### Post by gerowen on 2010-09-02

The XBox 360's online network is in a league of its own. I guess I've never really been a Microsoft "hater", I just "prefer" Linux because I find it easier to do a lot of my common tasks in a Linux environment after I got used to it.

### Post by Kixtosh on 2010-09-05
Personally, I have no hate whatsoever for Microsoft. I have been a happy user of W2K and Win XP Pro of late. Both performed very well, were very secure, and very stable. I had no BSOD and never really had to reinstall. So, why am I even here then?

Well, firstly, I mostly use ultra-portable laptops, weighing less than 3 lbs. Until very recently, this meant slow processors and slow (4,200 rpm) hard drives, as well as limited RAM. This presents some challenges. Firstly, my Windows installs would gradually get slower and slower to start, eventually taking about five minutes to boot up. This would mean that I would never turn them off, unless a new update required me to do so. I would simply suspend them in standby mode, and resume from there. It worked pretty well on the whole.

I've been interested in Linux since 2004 because of this, when I first tried Knoppix 3.7. It worked fine, but my wireless card  would not work back then, and I didn't know enough to fix it, so I held off until earlier this year again.

By then, support for W2K was ending, and I tried Ubuntu, Xubuntu, Puppy and some other stuff besides. My wireless worked in all of them, I got my boot times down to under one minute on my oldest machines (see signature), and they were usable again as "spares".

My main laptop, however, has since got infected, even though I never browse suspicious sites (porn, peer to peer downloads, ...) and I don't remember ever opening a suspicious e-mail attachment. It has been a pain to remove, however, and clean up. In the meantime, from trying Live CD's and Linux driven rescue disks, it looks as though one of the lightweight distros will work wonders on boot times and speed of operation for this newish laptop, so it's going to be re-installed soon, with dual boot XP Pro and Ubuntu. I'll only use XP when I have to probably (once bitten, twice shy, and all that sort of thing).

It was interesting to note, however, that Microsoft gave me fairly effective help for free because this was a security issue.

Actually, having recently purchased two cheap W7 laptops for my kids, I have to say that many issues seems to have been confronted. The modest laptops start fast, and seem to work well in general. Perhaps Microsoft have finally got the message!
